لأن المتكلمين يتشاركون فيه ، كما سنرى بعد ـ كما عرف قبل القرن الخامس . ولن أخوض في الأبحاث اللغوية لخروجها عن نطاق هذا البحث ، مع ملاحظة أن المباحث الأصولية اللغوية ليست من نوع علوم اللغة أو النحو العادية . فقد دقق الأصوليون نظرهم في فهم أشياء من كلام العرب لم يتوصل إليها اللغويون أو النحاة . إن كلام العرب متسع وطرق البحث فيه متشعبة . فكتب اللغة تضبط الألفاظ والمعاني الظاهرة دون المعاني الدقيقة التي يتوصل إليها الأصولي باستقراء يزيد على استقراء اللغوي . فهناك إذن دقائق لا يتعرض لها اللغوي ولا تقتضيها صناعة النحو . ولكن يتوصل إليها الأصوليون باستقراء خاص وأدلة خاصة(١) . هذا هو تاريخ المنهج الأصولي - استطعنا أن نتبين من خلاله موقف علماء أصول الفقه من المنطق الأرسططاليسي ، وتكون هذا المنهج منذ العصر الأول واستمر الأصوليون يكملون فيه ويضيفون إلى عناصره جدة في التفكير ، حتى وصل إلى أيدي الشافعي . فأقامه علماً متفق الأجزاء متناسق الأطراف . وفي كل هذا لم يتأثر على الإطلاق بمناهج البحث اليونانية - ثم انقسم إلى قسمين بعد الشافعي : علم الأصول الفقهي وعلم الأصول الكلامي . وفي كلا القسمين لم يتأثر أيضاً بمنطق أرسطو - حتى أتى القرن الخامس فمزج المسلمون المنطق الأرسططاليسي بالأصول ، وبهذا انتهى أو كاد ينتهي تفكير المسلمين المبدع في المنطق . هذا هو موقف علماء أصول الفقه - فقهاء كانوا أو متكلمين . فما هو موقف علماء أصول الدين المتكلمين من المنطق الأرسططاليسي ؟ هذا ما سنبحثه في الفصل القادم .
And he derived from the speech of Abu Hashim al-Jubbai on his selections, and indeed he departed from the method of the judge and his followers in places to the method of the Mu'tazila. Then his denial of God's knowledge of particulars, so just as he opposed the majority in all this, we see him also opposing them in his attempt to mix Aristotelian logic with usul. And he thereby paved the way for his student Abu Hamid al-Ghazali, which led to the predecessors attacking this latter one with a violent, harsh attack. And al-Ghazali is considered the true mixer of Aristotelian logic with the sciences of the Muslims. Not for what he wrote of easy-expressed logical books, but for that logical introduction which he placed at the beginning of his book al-Mustasfa, in which he mentioned that whoever does not encompass it, then his knowledge is definitely not trusted. And on this basis, Aristotle's logic was considered a condition for ijtihad and a collective obligation upon the Muslims, and due to this, severe objections were directed at al-Ghazali from the Muslim jurists. And from that time, the usuli theologians began to be influenced by Aristotelian logic and set apart in the beginnings of their books a special chapter for what they called 'theological introductions' or 'incoming introductions' in which they summarized Aristotle's logic or, more precisely, the logic of the Islamic commentators, sometimes Peripatetic and sometimes Stoic. And the most influenced by this Greek logic were the topics of words. As for the topic of qiyas, it continued, even in the hands of the later usulis, to be as far as possible from this Greek logic, whether Aristotelian or Stoic. And we will not present in these chapters those theological introductions. For we have already presented all types of Greek logic in the hands of its Islamic commentators. And those introductions are nothing but a form of these numerous commentaries written by the Muslims, whose elements I analyzed in the previous chapter. But we will present this usuli-kalami methodology – because the theologians share in it, as we will see later – as it was known before the fifth century. And I will not delve into linguistic researches because they are outside the scope of this study, noting that the linguistic usuli researches are not of the type of ordinary language sciences or grammar. For the usulis examined their view in understanding things from Arab speech that the linguists or grammarians did not arrive at. Indeed, Arab speech is vast and the methods of research in it are diverse. Language books record the apparent words and meanings without the precise meanings that the usuli arrives at through an induction that goes beyond the linguist's induction. So there are subtleties that the linguist does not address and that the craft of grammar does not require. But the usulis arrive at them through special induction and special evidence. This is the history of the usuli methodology – we were able to discern from it the position of the scholars of usul al-fiqh towards Aristotelian logic, that this methodology was formed since the first era and the usulis continued completing it and adding to its elements novelty in thinking, until it reached the hands of al-Shafi'i. So he established it as a science of consistent parts and coherent limbs. And in all this it was not influenced at all by Greek methods of study – then it split after al-Shafi'i into two branches: the science of fiqhi usul and the science of kalami usul. And in both branches it was also not influenced by Aristotle's logic – until the fifth century came and the Muslims mixed Aristotelian logic with usul, and with this ended, or almost ended, the creative thinking of the Muslims in logic. This is the position of the scholars of usul al-fiqh – whether jurists or theologians. So what is the position of the scholars of usul al-din (theologians) regarding Aristotelian logic? This is what we will research in the next chapter.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is a historical overview of the relationship between Aristotelian logic and the Islamic science of usul al-fiqh. The author begins by mentioning Abu Hashim al-Jubbai, who attempted to mix logic with usul, and notes his departure from the method of the judge (likely al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar) and his followers towards Mu'tazilite positions. He also denied God's knowledge of particulars, which placed him in opposition to the majority. The author then highlights al-Ghazali as the true mixer of Aristotelian logic with Islamic sciences, not merely through his simplified logical writings but because of the logical introduction he placed at the beginning of his work 'al-Mustasfa', where he declared that anyone who does not master logic cannot be trusted in his knowledge. This elevated Aristotelian logic to a condition for ijtihad and a collective obligation (fard kifaya). This provoked strong opposition from jurists. Subsequently, usuli theologians began to incorporate a chapter called 'theological introductions' (al-muqaddimat al-kalamiyya) or 'incoming introductions' in their works, summarizing Aristotle's logic as interpreted by Islamic commentators, sometimes Peripatetic, sometimes Stoic. The most affected area was the study of words (alfaz), while the study of analogy (qiyas) remained largely untouched by Greek logic. The author notes that he will not cover those theological introductions here, as he has already analyzed Greek logic in the hands of its Islamic commentators in a previous chapter. Instead, he will present the usuli-kalami methodology as it existed before the fifth Islamic century. He then addresses the distinct nature of linguistic usul researches, which go beyond ordinary language sciences and grammar; usulis have developed their own inductive methods to grasp subtle meanings not accessible to linguists. The passage concludes by summarizing the history: usul methodology was formed early and developed independently, was systematized by al-Shafi'i without Greek influence, split into fiqhi and kalami usul, still without Aristotelian influence until the fifth century when mixing occurred, which the author views as potentially stifling creative thinking. The passage ends by promising a discussion on the attitude of kalam theologians (usul al-din) towards Aristotelian logic in the next chapter.

Key Terms

القرن الخامس
المنهج الأصولي

Checkpoint
What does the passage state about the influence of Greek logic on usul methodology before the 5th century?
A) It was heavily influenced but later rejected.
B) It was partially influenced, especially in qiyas.
C) It was not influenced at all.
D) It was influenced only after al-Shafi'i.

Key Takeaway
Usul al-fiqh developed independently of Greek logic for centuries, and its integration in the 5th century marked a significant shift in Islamic intellectual history.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Select a passage from al-Shafi'i's al-Risala (e.g., on qiyas) and compare its reasoning style with a passage from al-Ghazali's al-Mustasfa (e.g., the logical introduction). Identify any differences in terminology and argument structure.
?
Based on the passage's claim that mixing ended creative thinking, discuss whether you agree or disagree, using examples from later usul works (e.g., al-Amidi, al-Subki).

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
The Early Mutakallimun's Independent Logical Method
Historical development of kalam's methodology vs. Aristotelian logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why early mutakallimun rejected Aristotelian logic and describe their own methodological framework based on usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
Kalam had its own logical method before Greek logic entered.

This lesson explores the historical position of early mutakallimun toward Aristotelian logic. You will see that they did not blindly accept Greek methods; instead, they developed a distinctive way of reasoning rooted in usul al-fiqh and special proofs. Understanding this independence is crucial for reading early kalam texts correctly.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the Dual Stance
The passage concludes that early mutakallimun had a dual position: rejection of Aristotelian logic and adoption of an Islamic method. Start by locating this conclusion in the final paragraph.
Look for 'أولاً – رفض المنطق... ثانياً – الأخذ بمنهاج إسلامي'
2
Gather Evidence for Rejection
The author provides several pieces of evidence: the anecdote of Yahya refusing debate, Ibn Taymiyya's statement about a special 'law', Ibn Khaldun's account of al-Baqillani's premises, and the mutakallimun's claim of independence. Identify each piece and note who says what.
Pay attention to the letters (د، هـ، و، ح) marking the points.
3
Understand the Alternative Method
The alternative method is described as a 'path' (tariqa) leading to knowledge, using 'special proofs' (adilla khassa) based on usul al-fiqh. Ibn Taymiyya contrasts it with the logicians' path. Summarize the key features of this method.
Ibn Taymiyya says: 'This path is not the path of the logicians; rather, it is special proofs.'
4
Identify the Turning Point
Ibn Khaldun notes that later scholars (mutakallimun) studied Aristotelian logic and revised many old premises, such as 'the invalidity of a proof indicates the invalidity of the proven'. This led to the 'method of the later ones' derived from Greek logic. Recognize this as the beginning of integration.
The turning point is associated with al-Juwayni and later commentators.

يقرر ابن تيمية ( ۷۲۹ هـ ) صراحة وجود قانون خاص امتاز به المتكلمون فيقول : « وكان للغزالي قانون هو المنطق - أما أبو بكر بن العربي فقد وضع قانوناً آخر مبنياً على طريقة أبي المعالي ومن قبله كالقاضي أبي بكر الباقلاني »(۳) ويذكر في فقرة أخرى أن نظار المسلمين عدلوا عن طريق المناطقة فقالوا « الطريق هو المرشد إلى المطلوب وهو الموصل إلى المقصود وهو ما يكون العلم ، (٤) وهذا الطريق غير طريق المناطقة بل هي أدلة خاصة ويردد ابن تيمية هذا في مواضع كثيرة من كتبه - In Baghdad one day, and in the gathering were some mutakallimun. The vizier asked them to debate with Yahya, but Yahya refused, saying: 'They do not understand the rules of my expressions, and I do not understand their terminology.' (1) D - Considering kalam itself as a method for the religious sciences, just as logic is a method for the rational sciences. For the commentator of al-Maqasid holds that among the most important reasons for which kalam was named is 'that it imparts the ability to speak in verification of religious matters, just as logic does for philosophical matters.' (2) E - Ibn Taymiyya (d. 729 AH) explicitly affirms the existence of a special law (qanun) distinctive to the mutakallimun, saying: 'Al-Ghazali had a law, which is logic. As for Abu Bakr ibn al-'Arabi, he laid down another law based on the method of Abu al-Ma'ali and before him al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani.' (3) And in another paragraph he mentions that the Muslim theoreticians diverged from the path of the logicians, saying: 'The path is that which guides to the sought, and it is that which leads to the intended, and it is that by which knowledge occurs.' (4) And this path is not the path of the logicians; rather, it is special proofs. Ibn Taymiyya repeats this in many places in his books, and Ibn al-Subki also repeats it. (5) F - What Ibn Khaldun (d. 808 AH / 1406 CE) mentions: that Abu Bakr al-Baqillani laid down the rational premises upon which proofs and considerations depend, among them 'that the invalidity of a proof indicates the invalidity of the proven,' and that this method was a theoretical art standing on its own. Then came Imam al-Haramayn, who composed in this method the book al-Shamil, and in this very book there is an explanation of the old method of the mutakallimun. Then he mentions that later scholars after this read Aristotelian logic, and in light of this logic they looked at the rules and premises in the art of kalam of the earlier ones, and they contradicted many of them. So they no longer believed that the invalidity of a proof indicates the invalidity of the proven. And another method was formed, the 'method of the later ones,' derived from Greek logic, whether Stoic or Aristotelian, and based upon it. (1) H - What the mutakallimun themselves hold: that kalam 'has no principles that are explained in another science; rather, it is independent in itself of what is other than it' (2). And al-Siyalkuti affirms that the investigations of theoretical reflection (nazar) contradict many of the Aristotelian logical issues (3). T - Ibn Rushd (d. 595 AH / 1198 CE) mentions that the mutakallimun had rhetorical methods and proofs (5). We conclude from the foregoing that the mutakallimun in the early kalam period had a dual position regarding the formation of method: First, rejection of Aristotelian logic as a method for research and attacking it. Second, adopting a special Islamic method laid down or whose foundations were laid by scholars of usul al-fiqh, and the mutakallimun dealt with it by addition and modification. And this method continued in the first period in all circles of the mutakallimun — whether Mu'tazila, Shi'a, Ash'aris, or Sunnis — isolated to the greatest extent from Aristotelian logic. As for the mixing of Aristotelian logic with kalam and the Islamic sciences in general, it began in the late
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is a historical narrative from a modern academic work on methodology. It presents evidence from various Islamic scholars to argue that kalam (Islamic theology) originally had its own methodological framework distinct from Aristotelian logic, and that the later integration was a gradual process. The author begins with an anecdote about a mutakallim named Yahya refusing to debate with logicians due to different terminologies, illustrating the separation. Then, point D cites the commentator of al-Maqasid to show that kalam itself was considered a method for religious sciences analogous to logic for rational sciences. Point E quotes Ibn Taymiyya affirming that the mutakallimun had a special 'law' (qanun) distinct from logic, mentioning al-Ghazali's logic and Abu Bakr ibn al-'Arabi's alternative method based on earlier scholars like al-Juwayni and al-Baqillani. Ibn Taymiyya describes their path as that which leads to knowledge, not the logicians' path but special proofs. This is repeated by Ibn al-Subki. Point F cites Ibn Khaldun's account: al-Baqillani laid rational premises for proofs, e.g., the invalidity of a proof indicates the invalidity of the proven. This method was a theoretical art. Later, Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni) wrote al-Shamil as an explanation of the old method. Later scholars then studied Aristotelian logic and revised many of the old rules, no longer accepting that premise. This led to the 'method of the later ones' based on Greek logic. Point H notes that the mutakallimun considered kalam independent with its own principles, and al-Siyalkuti says its investigations contradict Aristotelian logic. Point T mentions Ibn Rushd's observation that the mutakallimun used rhetorical proofs. The passage concludes that early mutakallimun had a dual stance: rejecting Aristotelian logic while adopting a special Islamic method based on usul al-fiqh, which remained isolated from Greek logic until later mixing began.

علم الكلام
المتكلمون
الطريقة

Key Takeaway
Early mutakallimun rejected Aristotelian logic and developed their own method based on usul al-fiqh, remaining isolated from Greek logic until later scholars integrated it.

The Dual Stance of Early Mutakallimun on Aristotelian Logic
Author describes early mutakallimun's rejection of Aristotelian logic and development of an Islamic methodology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the dual stance of early mutakallimun regarding Aristotelian logic and identify the source of their own methodology.

Core Idea
Early mutakallimun rejected Greek logic but built their own rigorous method from usul al-fiqh.

The early Islamic theologians (mutakallimun) did not simply dismiss logic; they developed a sophisticated methodology grounded in Islamic jurisprudence. This lesson examines their critical stance towards Aristotle's logic and their constructive alternative.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dual stance
Find the two positions: rejection of Aristotelian logic as a method and adoption of a specific Islamic methodology from usul al-fiqh.
2
Understand the reason for rejection
The author states that mutakallimun rejected Aristotle's metaphysics, thus they rejected his method based on it.
Note the metaphysical underpinning: they saw logic tied to Aristotelian metaphysics.
3
Recognize the Islamic methodology source
The Islamic method was laid down by scholars of usul al-fiqh and later adopted and expanded by mutakallimun.
This method was used by all early theological schools.
4
Note the historical shift
Al-Ghazali at the end of the fifth century initiated mixing Aristotelian logic with Islamic sciences, though opposition persisted.
This marks the transition to 'method of the later scholars'.

في نفسه عما عداه» ويقرر السيالكوتي أن مباحث النظر تخالف الكثير من المسائل المنطقية الأرسططاليسية. ط ـ يذكر ابن رشد أنه كان للمتكلمين طرق ودلائل خطابية. نستنتج مما تقدم أنه كان للمتكلمين في العصر الكلامي الأول حول تكوين المنهج موقف مزدوج. أولاً ـ رفض المنطق الارسططاليسي كمنهاج للبحث ومهاجمته. ثانياً ـ الأخذ بمنهاج إسلامي خاص وضعه أو وضع أسسه علماء أصول الفقه. وتناوله المتكلمون بالزيادة والتعديل. وقد استمر هذا المنهج في الفترة الأولى في جميع دوائر المتكلمين ـ معتزلة كانوا أو شيعة أو أشاعرة أو سنيين ـ بمعزل الى أكبر حد عن المنطق الأرسططاليسي. أما مزج المنطق الأرسططاليسي بعلم الكلام والعلوم الإسلامية على العموم فبدأ في اواخر القرن الخامس على أيدي المتأخرين من المتكلمين. غير أنه ينبغي أن نلاحظ أن هذا المزج لم يحدث على يد مفكر من المعتزلة، بل بواسطة متكلم أهل السنة المشهور أبي حامد الغزالي، ومع ذلك فقد بقي لمنطق أرسطو أعداء ممتازون حتى العصور الأخيرة، وسنبين هذا فيما بعد ـ وبقي لمناهج المتكلمين الأولين، أنصار كثيرون استخدموها واحتفظوا في جدلهم بها. ما هي العلة التي دفعت المتكلمين الى عدم قبول المنطق الأرسططاليسي؟ يمكنني أن أستنتج ببساطة أن العلة في هذا هو أن المتكلمين لم يقبلوا ميتافيزيقيا أرسطو. فكان من البديهي ألا يقبلوا منهاج البحث الذي استندت عليه هذه الميتافيزيقا. وسنحاول أن نعرض الآن لمنطق المتكلمين طبقاً لما لدينا من مصادر، مع ملاحظة أن هذا المنطق استخدمه الأصوليون ـ علماء أصول الفقه ـ قبل المتكلمين، ولذلك سنعبر عن علماء هاتين الفرقتين بالأصوليين ـ علماء أصول الفقه وعلماء أصول الدين ـ ثم نعرض بعد ذلك موقف المتكلمين من قانون بديهي هو قانون عدم ارتفاع النقيضين ـ الوسط الممتنع أو الثالث المرفوع ـ ثم نقدهم لمبدأ العلية. ثم نصل الى نقطة التحول من هذا المنطق الإسلامي الخاص الى منطق أرسطو.
Then he mentions that the later scholars after this read Aristotelian logic, and in light of this logic they examined the rules and premises in the art of kalam of the earlier scholars, so they contradicted many of them, and they no longer believed that the invalidity of the evidence indicates the invalidity of the indicated. And another method was formed, which is 'the method of the later scholars', derived from Greek logic, whether Stoic or Aristotelian, and based upon it. H – What the mutakallimun themselves opine: that the science of kalam 'has no principles elucidated in another science – rather it is self-sufficient from what is other than it.' And al-Siyalkuti affirms that the topics of theoretical investigation contradict many of the Aristotelian logical issues. T – Ibn Rushd mentions that the mutakallimun had rhetorical methods and proofs. We conclude from the foregoing that the mutakallimun in the first theological era had a dual stance regarding the formation of methodology. First: rejection of Aristotelian logic as a method for research and attacking it. Second: adopting a specific Islamic methodology laid down or whose foundations were laid by scholars of usul al-fiqh. The mutakallimun added to it and modified it. And this methodology continued in the first period among all circles of mutakallimun – whether Mu'tazila, Shi'a, Ash'aris, or Sunnis – isolated to the greatest extent from Aristotelian logic. As for the mixing of Aristotelian logic with kalam and Islamic sciences in general, it began at the end of the fifth century at the hands of the later mutakallimun. However, we should note that this mixing did not occur by a Mu'tazilite thinker, but by the famous Sunni mutakallim Abu Hamid al-Ghazali. Despite that, Aristotle's logic retained eminent enemies until recent times, and we will show this later. And many proponents of the first mutakallimun's methodologies remained, using them and preserving them in their dialectic. What is the cause that drove the mutakallimun to not accept Aristotelian logic? I can simply conclude that the cause for this is that the mutakallimun did not accept Aristotle's metaphysics. So it was self-evident that they would not accept the research method that this metaphysics was based upon. And we will now attempt to present the logic of the mutakallimun according to the sources we have, noting that this logic was used by the usuliyyun – scholars of usul al-fiqh – before the mutakallimun. Therefore, we will refer to the scholars of these two groups as the usuliyyun – scholars of usul al-fiqh and scholars of usul al-din. Then we will present thereafter the stance of the mutakallimun regarding the self-evident law of non-contradiction – the excluded middle or the raised third – then their critique of the principle of causality. Then we reach the turning point from this specific Islamic logic to Aristotle's logic.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage is a historical introduction to the relationship between early Islamic theologians (mutakallimun) and Aristotelian logic. The author begins by noting that later theologians read Aristotelian logic and used it to critique the premises of earlier kalam, leading to a new method called 'the method of the later scholars' derived from Greek logic. This marks a shift from earlier reliance on Islamic sources. The author then summarizes three points: (ح) the mutakallimun considered kalam self-sufficient and independent; (ط) Ibn Rushd noted their rhetorical methods. The conclusion is that early mutakallimun had a dual stance: rejecting Aristotelian logic while adopting a specifically Islamic methodology from usul al-fiqh. This Islamic method was used by all sects. The mixing of Aristotelian logic with Islamic sciences began in the late fifth century, notably by al-Ghazali, not a Mu'tazilite. Despite this, Aristotelian logic faced persistent opposition. The author attributes the initial rejection to the mutakallimun's rejection of Aristotle's metaphysics. Finally, the author outlines the plan to present the mutakallimun's logic, noting it was used by usul al-fiqh scholars first, and then to discuss their stance on the law of non-contradiction and causality, leading to the transition to Aristotelian logic.

Why Theologians Rejected Aristotelian Logic
Author explains why theologians rejected Aristotelian logic and outlines the upcoming discussion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the primary reason later Islamic theologians rejected Aristotelian logic and explain how this rejection shaped their methodological approach.

Core Idea
Theologians rejected Aristotelian logic because of its metaphysical underpinnings, not its formal structure.

The adoption of Aristotelian logic in the Islamic world was not uniform. While philosophers embraced it, many theologians, especially among the Ash'aris, viewed it with suspicion. This passage from a modern study of Islamic logic explains the core reason: the theologians did not accept Aristotle's metaphysics, and thus could not accept a methodology built upon it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key groups
The passage mentions 'mutakallimun' (later theologians) and 'usuliyyun' (scholars of usul). Note that the author uses 'usuliyyun' to include both legal theorists and theologians. Understand that the rejection discussed is primarily from the mutakallimun.
2
Find the cause of rejection
The author states directly: 'the cause is that the theologians did not accept Aristotle's metaphysics.' This is the central claim. Aristotle's logic was seen as inseparable from his metaphysical framework.
The author considers this a self-evident reason: if you reject the foundation, you reject what is built on it.
3
Recognize the historical context
The passage notes that this rejection persisted despite al-Ghazali's integration of logic into Sunni theology. This shows that even after al-Ghazali, many theologians remained opposed.
This sets up a tension: al-Ghazali is seen as a bridge figure, but the opposition continued.
4
Identify the upcoming discussion topics
The author outlines the plan: first, present the logic of the theologians; second, discuss their stance on the law of non-contradiction and the principle of causality; finally, describe the transition to Aristotelian logic.
This preview helps you understand the structure of the book and the key issues at stake.
5
Connect to broader Islamic sciences
The rejection of Aristotelian causality, for example, directly impacts theological debates about divine action and occasionalism (as held by Ash'aris). Knowing this helps you analyze arguments in kalam.
This is a foundational tool for reading theological works with logical awareness.

The fifth century at the hands of the later theologians. However, it should be noted that this mixing did not occur by a Mu'tazilite thinker, but by the famous Sunni theologian Abu Hamid al-Ghazali. Nevertheless, Aristotelian logic had prominent enemies even until recent times, and we will show this later. And the methods of the earlier theologians retained many supporters who used them and preserved them in their dialectic. What is the cause that drove the theologians to reject Aristotelian logic? I can simply conclude that the cause is that the theologians did not accept Aristotle's metaphysics. So it was self-evident that they would not accept the method of inquiry based on this metaphysics. And we will now attempt to present the logic of the theologians according to the sources we have, noting that this logic was used by the usuliyyun—scholars of usul al-fiqh—before the theologians, and therefore we will refer to the scholars of both groups as usuliyyun—scholars of usul al-fiqh and scholars of usul al-din. Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is a historical and methodological introduction. It begins by noting that the fusion of logic and theology in the 5th century AH was not initiated by Mu'tazilites but by the Ash'ari theologian Abu Hamid al-Ghazali. Nonetheless, Aristotelian logic remained objectified by some. The author argues that the primary reason for the theologians' rejection of Aristotelian logic was their rejection of Aristotle's metaphysics. Since they did not accept the metaphysical premises, they naturally rejected the logical methodology built upon them. The author then outlines the plan of the work: to present the logic of the theologians (mutakallimun) using available sources, noting that this logic was actually used by usuliyyun (scholars of legal theory) before the theologians, so both groups are collectively referred to as usuliyyun. The discussion will cover the theologians' stance on the law of non-contradiction (excluded middle) and their critique of causality, leading to the transition from this indigenous Islamic logic to Aristotelian logic.

Classification of Islamic Reasoning Sources
Author introduces chapter on Islamic reasoning, classifying sources.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the sources of Islamic reasoning into transmitted and weak categories and explain the role of qiyas usuli.

Core Idea
Islamic reasoning divides into fully transmitted qiyas usuli and scattered weak methods.

This passage opens the fourth chapter on Islamic reasoning, which is the second part of Islamic logic. The author distinguishes between two types of reasoning sources: those fully transmitted to us (qiyas usuli) and those only found in fragmented discussions where later scholars criticized their weakness (al-turuq al-da'ifa). Understanding this classification is key to recognizing which arguments are reliable in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main types of reasoning sources
Read the passage and locate where the author divides the sources of Islamic reasoning topics into two types. Note the Arabic terms and their English equivalents.
The division is explicit in the sentence starting with 'تنقسم مصادر هذه المباحث إلى نوعين'.
2
Distinguish between transmitted and weak methods
For the first type, note its name (qiyas usuli) and that its sources have fully reached us. For the second type, note its name (al-turuq al-da'ifa) and that it has not been directly transmitted, only through critical later works.
The weak methods were discussed only to show their weakness, not to present them as valid methods.
3
Identify the alternative terminology used by mutakallimun
Find the phrase 'qiyas al-shahid 'ala al-gha'ib' which is the mutakallimun's term for qiyas usuli. Consider what this analogy ('from the present to the absent') implies about the reasoning process.
This term emphasizes reasoning from the known (shahid) to the unknown (gha'ib).
4
Understand the author's focus on usuliyyun's views
The author states that in this chapter, only the views of usul al-fiqh scholars will be presented, not those of the mutakallimun. Identify the reason given: they agree on the essence but differ in presentation and added elements.
The mutakallimun's views will be covered in the next chapter.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفَصْلُ الرَّابِعُ
مباحث الاستدلال الإسلامية
- القياس الأصولي -
مباحث الإستدلال الإسلامية أو أدلة العقول هي القسم الثاني من المنطق الإسلامي . وقد اعتبرها المسلمون بمثابة مباحث الاستدلال عند الأرسططاليسيين فكانت مناهجاً للبحث العلمي استخدمها الأصوليون علماء الكلام وعلماء أصول الفقه . صور الأولون بها حججهم واستنبط الآخرون بواسطتها أحكامهم . وتنقسم مصادر هذه المباحث إلى نوعين : نوع وصلت إلينا مصادره وهو القياس الأصولي ، ونوع لم تصل إلينا ولا نستطيع أن نعرضه إلا خلال أبحاث متناثرة بقيت في كتب المتأخرين - متكلمين كانوا أو أصوليين - لم يكن المقصود منها عرض هذه المناهج أو هذه الأدلة بالذات ، بل إظهار ضعفها وعدم أخذ المتأخرين بها . ولذلك أسماها المتأخرون بالطرق الضعيفة . وسنفرد هذا الفصل للقياس الأصولي ، وهو ما يسميه المتكلمون بقياس الشاهد على الغائب . غير أننا لن نبحث في آراء المتكلمين عن القياس ، في هذا الفصل بل سنقصر عرضنا فيه على آراء الأصوليين علماء أصول الفقه ، وليس السبب في هذا أن المتكلمين وعلماء أصول الفقه يختلفون في جوهر القياس بل بالعكس إنهم يتفقون في جوهر الفكرة . ولكن لأنهم يختلفون في طريقة العرض وفي أن كلاً من علماء الكلام وأصول الفقه يضيف عناصر لم يضفها علماء الفرقة الأخرى . أما آراء المتكلمين في قياس الغالب على الشاهد وما أضافوه من عناصر ، لم يوافق عليها الأصوليون -
علماء أصول الفقه ـ فسنعرض لها في الفصل القادم مع بقية مدارك العقول الأخرى .
The fourth chapter: Islamic Reasoning Topics - Qiyas Usuli. Islamic reasoning topics or rational proofs are the second part of Islamic logic. Muslims considered them as the topics of reasoning among the Aristotelians, so they were methods for scientific research used by usuliyyun, mutakallimun, and usul al-fiqh scholars. The former formed their proofs with them, and the latter deduced their rulings through them. The sources of these topics are divided into two types: a type whose sources have reached us, which is qiyas usuli, and a type that has not reached us and we cannot present it except through scattered research remaining in the books of later scholars - whether mutakallimun or usuliyyun - whose purpose was not to present these methods or these proofs themselves, but rather to show their weakness and that later scholars did not adopt them. Therefore, later scholars called them 'weak methods'. We will devote this chapter to qiyas usuli, which the mutakallimun call 'qiyas al-shahid 'ala al-gha'ib' (analogy from the present to the absent). However, we will not discuss the opinions of the mutakallimun about qiyas in this chapter, but will restrict our presentation to the opinions of the usuliyyun, the scholars of usul al-fiqh. The reason for this is not that the mutakallimun and usul al-fiqh scholars differ in the essence of qiyas; on the contrary, they agree in the essence of the idea. But because they differ in the method of presentation and in that each of the mutakallimun and usul al-fiqh scholars adds elements that the other group did not add. As for the opinions of the mutakallimun on qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid and what they added of elements that the usuliyyun - the scholars of usul al-fiqh - did not agree upon, we will present them in the next chapter along with the rest of the other intellectual sources.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage is the introduction to the fourth chapter of the book, which deals with Islamic reasoning methods, specifically qiyas usuli (analogical reasoning in usul al-fiqh). The author begins by stating that Islamic reasoning topics constitute the second part of Islamic logic. Muslims considered them equivalent to the topics of reasoning in Aristotelian logic, but they developed them as methods for scientific research used by both mutakallimun (theologians) and usul al-fiqh scholars. The mutakallimun used them to form their proofs, while the usuliyyun used them to deduce legal rulings. The author then classifies the sources of these topics into two types: one that has been transmitted to us in full (qiyas usuli) and another that has not been transmitted directly, but only through scattered discussions in later books where these methods were criticized as weak. Hence, they are called 'al-turuq al-da'ifa' (the weak methods). The chapter will focus on qiyas usuli, which the mutakallimun term 'qiyas al-shahid 'ala al-gha'ib' (analogy from the witnessed to the unseen). The author explains that within this chapter, only the views of the usul al-fiqh scholars will be presented, not those of the mutakallimun, because the two groups differ in presentation style and additional elements, though they agree on the essence of qiyas. The mutakallimun's views will be covered in the next chapter.

Key Terms
القياس الأصولي
الطرق الضعيفة
متكلمون
أصوليون

Key Takeaway
Islamic reasoning sources are classified into fully transmitted qiyas usuli and weak, scattered methods, with qiyas usuli being the principal method used in usul al-fiqh.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
5 min
Al-Ghazali and the Integration of Aristotelian Logic into Islamic Sciences
Historical overview of Ghazali's integration of Aristotelian logic

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the historical shift from indigenous Islamic logic to Aristotelian logic in the 5th century AH and explain al-Ghazali's positive assessment of Aristotelian logic and its purpose.

Core Idea
Al-Ghazali's acceptance of Aristotelian logic changed the course of Islamic intellectual history.

Prior to the 5th century AH, Muslim scholars of kalam and fiqh had developed their own logical methods, distinct from Aristotle. In the mid-5th century, a remarkable shift occurred: Muslims began to adopt Aristotelian logic into their sciences. Historians of Islamic thought nearly unanimously credit al-Ghazali with this integration. But what exactly did al-Ghazali think about Aristotelian logic? And how did his view fit into his broader intellectual project? This lesson unpacks al-Ghazali's pioneering role and his controversial yet consistent stance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the pre-Ghazali context
Look at the passage's opening: the usuliyyun (scholars of usul al-din and usul al-fiqh) rejected Aristotelian logic and developed their own. Note the timeline: this continued until the mid-5th century AH. This sets the stage for change.
The usuliyyun are not just jurists but also theologians. Their rejection was not total ignorance but a deliberate intellectual choice.
2
Recognize the turning point
Find where the passage says 'in that century, Muslims began to use Aristotelian logic in their sciences'. The mid-5th century is described as a 'dividing line' between two eras. Identify who is credited: al-Ghazali is unanimously seen as the initiator.
This historical consensus is important: it shows the magnitude of al-Ghazali's influence.
3
Analyze al-Ghazali's classification of sciences
In the passage, al-Ghazali in his early work 'Maqasid al-Falasifa' divides Greek sciences into four: mathematics, theology, logic, and physics. Logic is a separate category. Note that he separates logic from philosophy proper, which likely eases its acceptance.
This classification is not merely descriptive; it strategically isolates logic as a value-neutral tool.
4
Extract al-Ghazali's assessment of logic and its purpose
The passage directly quotes al-Ghazali: logic is 'mostly on the path of correctness, error rare', and disputes are only 'terminological'. Its purpose is to 'refine methods of inferential reasoning' and ultimately to 'distinguish knowledge from ignorance', which perfects the soul. Understand that for al-Ghazali, logic serves both intellectual and spiritual ends.
This dual purpose – inference and soul-perfection – is a key Ghazalian contribution.

Chapter Eight: The blending of Aristotelian logic with Muslim sciences by al-Ghazali and al-Ghazali's stance on Aristotelian logic. We saw in the previous chapters that the usuliyyun – scholars of the principles of religion and scholars of the principles of jurisprudence – did not accept Aristotelian logic. They developed a logic that contradicted this logic in its essence. The usuliyyun persisted in this stance until the middle of the fifth century AH. In that century, Muslims began to use Aristotelian logic in their sciences, such that the middle of the fifth century is considered a critical dividing line between two precise eras: an era in which Muslims did not resort to blending their sciences with Greek logic and philosophy, and an era in which Muslims began this blending, especially in the realm of logic. Muslim scholars almost unanimously agree that this latter movement was initiated by al-Ghazali. This calls us to investigate al-Ghazali's stance on Aristotelian logic and to determine his idea of it. Determining al-Ghazali's final thought on any subject he treated is extremely difficult – because al-Ghazali's thought underwent violent intellectual changes – unless we consider that his later works express his final opinion. His enemies noticed the changes in al-Ghazali's thought when they examined his works and attacked him violently. We see this clearly in the historical introduction written by Ibn Tufayl in his book Hayy ibn Yaqzan (1), and we also see this with Ibn Sab'in (1) and Ibn Taymiyya (2). However, these criticisms were specific to al-Ghazali's philosophical-religious opinions. As for his logical opinions, Muslims – except Ibn Taymiyya – say that his idea about logic never changed at all. Here we encounter another problem: Do al-Ghazali's various logical works represent specific viewpoints, such that each one expresses a viewpoint that al-Ghazali intended to present? Or are all his logical works merely a declaration in the heart of the Islamic world of a certain idea, namely, Aristotle's logic? Or did the man, being the genius of the Islamic world and the master of its thinkers, intend to present worldly sciences to Muslims and put each one to the test, and his analysis of logic was his only mistake in his lofty worldly journey? We will in any case present examples of his general views in these books. We will see to what extent this statement is true. Al-Ghazali attempts, in the introduction to al-Maqasid – which is the first thing he wrote in logic as far as we know – to present the Greek sciences, dividing them into four parts: 'Mathematics, Theology, Logic, and Physics.' Al-Ghazali's idea about logic is that 'most of it is on the path of correctness and error is rare in it; they (the Greeks) only oppose the people of truth in terminologies and objections, not in meanings and purposes. For its aim is to refine the methods of inferential reasoning, and that is something shared by all thinkers.' (3) Al-Ghazali repeats this also in al-Tahafut (4). The benefit of Aristotelian logic according to al-Ghazali is not limited to reaching the unknown from the known, but includes distinguishing knowledge from ignorance, and distinguishing knowledge from ignorance means perfecting the soul and its happiness. (5) And this idea was not ...
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is a historical and analytical introduction to Chapter Eight of the book. The author first sets the historical context: prior to the fifth century AH, scholars of usul (both kalam and fiqh) rejected Aristotelian logic and developed their own alternative logic. However, in the mid-fifth century, Muslims began to integrate Aristotelian logic into their sciences, a shift attributed primarily to al-Ghazali. The author then notes the difficulty of determining al-Ghazali's final position due to his intellectual evolution, and mentions criticisms from figures like Ibn Tufayl, Ibn Sab'in, and Ibn Taymiyya—though these criticisms targeted his philosophical-religious views, not his logical stance. Significantly, the author states that most Muslims (except Ibn Taymiyya) maintain that al-Ghazali's view on logic never changed. This raises a problem: should al-Ghazali's various logical works be seen as expressing different viewpoints, or are they all endorsing Aristotelian logic? The author then introduces al-Ghazali's early work 'Maqasid al-Falasifa' (The Aims of the Philosophers), in which he classifies Greek sciences into four categories: mathematics, theology, logic, and physics. Al-Ghazali asserts that logic is mostly correct, with errors rare; any opposition to the truth is merely terminological, not substantive. The purpose of logic is to refine methods of inference, a task shared by all thinkers. Finally, the passage highlights that for al-Ghazali, the benefit of logic extends beyond inference to distinguishing knowledge from ignorance, which perfects the soul and leads to happiness. This passage thus provides a broad overview of Ghazali's role in introducing Aristotelian logic to Islamic sciences, his positive evaluation of logic, and the historical debates surrounding his thought.

Key Takeaway

The Jurists' Stance on Logic After the 5th Century AH
Chapter heading introducing the jurists' stance on logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the central topic of the third chapter and explain why the jurists' stance on Aristotelian logic is a critical issue in Islamic intellectual history.

Core Idea
The jurists' engagement with logic shaped the methodologies of usul al-fiqh and kalam.

This chapter heading marks a pivot in the book: from the history of logic to its reception by Islamic jurists. After the 5th century AH, fuqaha began to explicitly address the value and danger of Aristotelian logic. This lesson sets the stage for understanding that debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the heading carefully
Notice the three key terms: fuqaha (jurists), mantiq (logic), and Arisṭāṭālīsī (Aristotelian). What does each term refer to? Why might the author specify 'Aristotelian' rather than just 'logic'?
2
Recall the historical context
Why after the 5th century AH? What had changed in the Islamic world regarding Greek sciences? Consider the translation movement, the rise of independent ijtihad, and figures like al-Ghazali.
3
Predict the chapter's likely content

4
Connect to your own studies
How might the jurists' stance affect your understanding of fiqh or usul? For example, why would some jurists resist logic while others embrace it?

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate

Al-Ghazali's Integration of Aristotelian Logic into Islamic Sciences
Author discusses Al-Ghazali's influence integrating Aristotelian logic into Islamic sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key changes Al-Ghazali's movement brought to usul scholarship, specifically in definitions and the shift from analogical reasoning to categorical syllogism.

Core Idea
Al-Ghazali introduced Aristotelian syllogism into Islamic sciences, transforming definitions and argumentation.

This lesson explores a pivotal moment in Islamic intellectual history: Al-Ghazali's integration of Aristotelian logic into usul al-fiqh and kalam. The passage sets the stage for understanding how scholars of usul began to replace traditional Islamic proofs with formal logical structures, redefining terms like 'substance' and 'intoxicant' with precise, fixed meanings. By the end, you will grasp why this shift matters for distinguishing certainty (yaqin) from conjecture (zann) in Islamic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Locate Al-Ghazali's project
Read the first few lines: the author mentions Al-Ghazali's movement that mixed Aristotle's logic with Islamic sciences. Note his initial promotion and later caution about contradictions.
Al-Ghazali is both the catalyst and later a critic.
2
Trace the scholars' response
Identify that usul scholars (usul al-din and usul al-fiqh) continued Al-Ghazali's earlier call, integrating logic into their fields.
The passage says they 'followed what Al-Ghazali called for in his many earlier books.'
3
Examine the impact on definitions (hudud)
Notice that scholars began formulating definitions 'in the manner of Aristotelian logic' – aiming for fixed, precise significations. This led to rejecting older definitions.
Examples: 'ayn (essence), jawhar (substance), 'arad (accident), khamr (wine).
4
Distinguish between two types of qiyas
Find the contrast between 'qiyas tamthili' (analogical reasoning, yielding conjecture) and 'qiyas mantiqi' (logical syllogism, yielding certainty). Scholars started using the latter to formulate arguments.
This distinction is crucial for understanding later debates, including Ibn Taymiyya's critique.

Chapter One: The stance of jurists towards Aristotelian logic until the era of Ibn Taymiyya. We spoke in the last chapter of the second section about the intellectual movement that Al-Ghazali initiated in Islamic logical thought, which led to mixing Aristotle's logic with the sciences of Muslims. And although Al-Ghazali finally realized what contradictions the application of Aristotelian logic to Muslim theology might produce, the scholars of usul (usul al-din and usul al-fiqh) continued what Al-Ghazali called for in his many earlier books of mixing Aristotelian logic with usul al-fiqh and kalam. It replaced the proofs of Islamic inquiry, and Aristotelian logic penetrated into the core of the theological and usuli issues themselves. From this arose an intellectual movement that had a strong impact on Islamic thought. Scholars of usul began to formulate their definitions in the manner of Aristotelian logic, and attempted to define their terminology on this basis, and provided clear definitions for them. This led to the rejection of many of the earlier definitions of theologians and usulis. Thus the term 'ayn (essence) or jawhar (substance), and the term 'arad (accident), and other theological definitions, and the term qara', and the term khamr (wine) or muskir (intoxicant), and other usuli definitions, must have fixed, precise significations. Moreover, the topics of logic entered into all linguistic and usuli investigations. As for the theologians or scholars of usul al-din, they began to speak about conjecture in analogical reasoning (qiyas tamthili) and about certainty in logical syllogism (qiyas mantiqi), and they began to formulate their arguments in the form of the latter syllogism.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage is a historical introduction to the first chapter, which discusses the stance of jurists towards Aristotelian logic up to the era of Ibn Taymiyya. The author begins by referencing the previous chapter about Al-Ghazali's intellectual movement that merged Aristotelian logic with Islamic sciences. Although Al-Ghazali later recognized potential contradictions in applying Aristotelian logic to theology, scholars of usul (both usul al-din and usul al-fiqh) continued his earlier project. This led to Aristotelian logic replacing traditional Islamic proofs and penetrating deeply into theological and jurisprudential issues. The movement caused scholars to rework their definitions (hudud) according to Aristotelian methods, aiming for clear, fixed meanings. Consequently, many older definitions by theologians and usulis were rejected. The passage gives examples: 'ayn or jawhar (substance), 'arad (accident), qara' (possibly a specific term, OCR unclear), and khamr/muskir (wine/intoxicant) — all needed precise definitions. Additionally, logic entered linguistic and usuli research. Theologians began distinguishing between conjecture (zann) in analogical reasoning (qiyas tamthili, the Islamic form of analogy) and certainty (yaqin) in logical syllogism (qiyas mantiqi, the Aristotelian categorical syllogism), and they started formulating their arguments in the latter form. The author is setting the historical context for Ibn Taymiyya's critique.

Key Terms
المنطق الأرسططاليسي (al-mantiq al-arisṭāṭālīsī)
أصول الفقه (uṣūl al-fiqh)
القياس التمثيلي (al-qiyās al-tamthīlī)
القياس المنطقي (al-qiyās al-manṭiqī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Definitions remained unchanged because they were based on revelation.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali's integration of Aristotelian logic reshaped Islamic sciences by introducing fixed definitions and prioritizing categorical syllogism over analogical reasoning, setting the stage for later debates.

Ibn Taymiyya's Four Maqamat: Negative and Positive Positions
Outlines Ibn Taymiyya's four maqamat and the author's analytical framework for studying his critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the four maqamat (two negative, two positive) in Ibn Taymiyya's critique of logicians.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya dismantles the logicians' exclusivity claims through four critical positions.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya outlines the structure of his critique. He identifies four maqamat: two negative claims that only definition and syllogism yield knowledge, and two positive claims that definition and syllogism do yield knowledge. Understanding this framework is essential for following his detailed refutations.

Guided Path
1
State the logicians' foundational claim
Logicians assert that knowledge is either concept or assent, attained only through definition and syllogism respectively.
2
Enumerate the four maqamat
Ibn Taymiyya breaks their claim into four positions: two negative (exclusivity of definition and syllogism) and two positive (efficacy of definition and syllogism).
The negative positions deny other paths; the positive positions affirm their own methods.
3
Explain each maqam
First negative: concept is attained only by definition. Second negative: assent is attained only by syllogism. First positive: definition yields concepts. Second positive: syllogism yields assents.
4
Understand Ibn Taymiyya's verdict
Ibn Taymiyya declares all four claims false: neither are non-logical paths entirely invalid nor are logical paths entirely correct as claimed.
5
Preview the unsystematic critique
Ibn Taymiyya does not follow a consistent order, mixing destructive and constructive arguments, prompting the author's framework.
This sets the stage for the destructive-constructive analysis.

ويبين ابن تيمية النهج الذي سيسير عليه في نقده بقوله « إعلم أنهم بنوا المنطق على الكلام في الحد ونوعه . قالوا لأن العلم إما تصور وإما تصديق . فالطريق الذي ينال به التصور هو الحد والطريق الذي ينال به التصديق هو القياس . فنقول : الكلام في أربعة مقامات - مقامين سالبين ومقامين موجبين - فالأولان في قولهم إن التصور المطلوب لا ينال إلا بالحد . والثاني إن التصديق المطلوب لا ينال إلا بالقياس . والآخران في أن الحد يفيد العلم بالتصورات وأن القياس أو البرهان الموصوف يفيد العلم بالتصديقات . فالمقامات السالبان ينفيان الطرق التي يسلكها غير المناطقة في التوصل إلى التصور والتصديق . والمقامان الموجبان يثبتان أن طريقي المناطقة هما وحدهما يؤديان إلى التصور والتصديق . ويرى ابن تيمية أن « كل هذه الدعاوي كذب في النفي والإثبات فلا ما نفوه من طرق غيرهم كلها باطل ولا ما أثبتوه من طرقهم كلها حق على الوجه الذي ادعوا فيه » ثم يبدأ ابن تيمية بنقد كل مقام من هذه المقامات . ولكن ابن تيمية لا يلتزم ترتيباً معيناً في نقد كل من المقام السالب والمقام الموجب للحد والقياس - فمثلاً في نقده للمقام السالب للحد لا يورد شيئاً من آرائه الخاصة عن الحد ـ كما يفهمه المسلمون . بل يجعل هذا المقام نقداً صرفاً للحد الأرسططاليسي . وفي المقام الموجب للحد يورد آراءه هو عن الحد . ولكنه في المقام السالب للقياس يورد أغلب آرائه عن صور الإستدلال القرآني . ويفرد معظم المقام الموجب في نقد القياس وغيره من صور الإستدلال الأرسططاليسي . ويلاحظ أيضاً على ابن تيمية أنه ينقد المقام السالب للحد بحجج لا يرددها في المقام الموجب ، لكنه ينقد المقام السالب للقياس بما نقد به المقام الموجب من حجج . وعدم وجود النظام الدقيق في كتابات ابن تيمية من ناحية ، واختلاط المسائل وتكرارها من ناحية أخرى ، يدعونا إلى وضع منهاج نسير عليه في دراستنا لفكر ابن تيمية المنطقي . أما هذا المنهج فهو تقسيم نقد ابن تيمية إلى جانبين : جانب هادم وجانب إنشائي ، فنورد في الجانب الأول نقد ابن تيمية الهدمي للمقامين السالب والموجب لكل من الحد والقياس . وفي الجانب الثاني ، آراء ابن تيمية في الحد الإسلامي وفي صور الإستدلال القرآنية - مستخلصين بعض تلك الآراء من كتب ابن تيمية الكلامية وغير الكلامية .
Ibn Taymiyya clarifies the approach he will follow in his critique, saying: 'Know that they built logic on discussion of definition (hadd) and its type. They said: because knowledge is either concept (tasawwur) or assent (tasdiq). The path by which concept is attained is definition, and the path by which assent is attained is syllogism (qiyas). So we say: the discussion is in four positions (maqamat) - two negative (salibayn) and two positive (mujibayn). The first two concern their statement that the desired concept is attained only by definition. The second that the desired assent is attained only by syllogism. The last two concern that definition yields knowledge of concepts, and that syllogism or the described demonstration yields knowledge of assents. So the negative positions negate the paths that non-logicians follow in reaching concept and assent. The positive positions affirm that the logicians' two paths alone lead to concept and assent.' Ibn Taymiyya holds that 'all these claims are false in negation and affirmation: neither what they negate of others' paths are all false, nor what they affirm of their own paths are all true in the manner they claimed.' Then Ibn Taymiyya begins to critique each of these positions. But Ibn Taymiyya does not adhere to a specific order in critiquing each of the negative and positive positions for definition and syllogism. For example, in his critique of the negative position on definition, he does not present any of his own views on definition - as Muslims understand it. Rather, he makes this position a pure critique of Aristotelian definition. And in the positive position on definition, he presents his own views on definition. But in the negative position on syllogism, he presents most of his views on the forms of Qur'anic inference. And he devotes most of the positive position to critiquing the syllogism and other forms of Aristotelian inference. It is also noted about Ibn Taymiyya that he critiques the negative position on definition with arguments that he does not repeat in the positive position, but he critiques the negative position on syllogism with the same arguments he used for the positive position. The lack of precise order in Ibn Taymiyya's writings, on the one hand, and the mixing and repetition of issues, on the other, calls us to establish a method that we follow in our study of Ibn Taymiyya's logical thought. This method is to divide Ibn Taymiyya's critique into two aspects: a destructive aspect and a constructive aspect. In the first aspect, we present Ibn Taymiyya's destructive critique of the negative and positive positions for both definition and syllogism. In the second aspect, we present Ibn Taymiyya's views on Islamic definition and the forms of Qur'anic inference - extracting some of these views from Ibn Taymiyya's theological and non-theological books.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This is a methodological passage from a contemporary study on Ibn Taymiyya's critique of logic. The author begins by quoting Ibn Taymiyya's own description of the logicians' foundational claims. Logicians, according to Ibn Taymiyya, built logic on definition and its type, arguing that knowledge is either concept (tasawwur) or assent (tasdiq), attained only through definition and syllogism respectively. Ibn Taymiyya counters that the discussion involves four positions: two negative and two positive. The first two reject the exclusivity of definition for concepts and syllogism for assent; the last two affirm that definition and syllogism do yield knowledge, but not as the logicians claim. Ibn Taymiyya declares all these claims false in both their negations and affirmations: neither are all non-logical paths invalid, nor are the logical paths entirely correct in the manner claimed. The author then observes that Ibn Taymiyya's critique lacks systematic order: he critiques the negative position on definition with purely destructive arguments without offering his own views, reserving his positive views for the positive position; but for syllogism, he presents his own Qur'anic alternatives when critiquing the negative position. Moreover, he reuses the same arguments for both negative and positive positions on syllogism. This lack of order and repetition leads the author to propose a two-fold analytical framework: a destructive aspect covering Ibn Taymiyya's refutation of the four positions, and a constructive aspect presenting his own positive views on Islamic definition and Qur'anic inference, drawn from his theological works. This framework structures the subsequent chapters.

Key Terms
مقام (maqam): A position or claim in the logical debate; specifically, one of four propositions that Ibn Taymiyya responds to: two negations and two affirmations about definition and syllogism. [Note: In Sufi contexts, maqam means spiritual station; here it means a discursive proposition.]
سالب (salib): A negative claim; here, the claim that non-logical methods do not yield knowledge, i.e., that definition and syllogism are the exclusive paths.
موجب (mujib): A positive claim; here, the claim that definition and syllogism do yield knowledge of concepts and assents.
هدمي (hadmi): Destructive; referring to Ibn Taymiyya's critique that refutes the logicians' positions without offering alternatives.
إنشائي (insha'i): Constructive; referring to the aspect of Ibn Taymiyya's thought that presents his own positive doctrines on definition and inference.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does Ibn Taymiyya characterize the logicians' claims about definition and syllogism?
A) They are entirely true in both affirmation and negation.
B) They are false only in their negative aspects.
C) They are false in both affirmation and negation.
D) They are true only in their positive aspects.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya rejects the logicians' exclusivity claims in all four maqamat.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given a statement by Ibn Taymiyya, determine which of the four maqamat it corresponds to. Example: 'The logicians claim that definition is the only path to concepts, but this is false.' (Answer: first negative maqam).
?
List the four maqamat and provide a one-sentence description of each, using your own words.

Introduction and Front Matter
advanced
5 min
The Author's Analytical Framework: Destructive and Constructive Aspects
Outlines Ibn Taymiyya's four maqamat and the author's analytical framework for studying his critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the destructive-constructive (hadmi-insha'i) framework to classify Ibn Taymiyya's arguments.

Core Idea
To navigate Ibn Taymiyya's disorderly critique, scholars separate his destructive refutation from his constructive Islamic alternative.

The passage observes that Ibn Taymiyya does not follow a consistent order in his critique: he treats definition and syllogism differently, mixing destructive and constructive arguments. To bring clarity, the author proposes a two-aspect framework: destructive (hadmi) and constructive (insha'i). This lesson teaches you to distinguish these aspects and apply them to Ibn Taymiyya's works.

Guided Path
1
Observe the lack of order
Ibn Taymiyya critiques the negative position on definition purely destructively, but the positive position on definition constructively. For syllogism, he mixes both aspects.
2
Note the inconsistency
He uses different arguments for definition's negative and positive maqamat, but reuses arguments for syllogism's maqamat.
This mixing of arguments across maqamat creates confusion.
3
Identify the need for a framework
The author argues that this unsystematic approach requires a clear method to study Ibn Taymiyya's logical thought.
4
Define the destructive aspect
The destructive aspect (hadmi) covers Ibn Taymiyya's refutations of the logicians' positions without offering his own alternatives.
Example: his pure critique of Aristotelian definition in the negative maqam.
5
Define the constructive aspect
The constructive aspect (insha'i) presents Ibn Taymiyya's own positive views on Islamic definition and Qur'anic inference.
Example: his views on definition in the positive maqam and his Qur'anic inference forms.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
5 min
The Structure of Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Aristotelian Syllogistic
Author outlines the fourth chapter's scope: Ibn Taymiyya's formal critique of Aristotelian syllogisms, including destructive and constructive aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the destructive and constructive aspects of Ibn Taymiyya's critique and identify the formal focus of his attack on Aristotelian syllogistic.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya did not reject all syllogisms; he dismantled Aristotelian demonstration and built an Islamic alternative.

This lesson introduces the overarching plan of Ibn Taymiyya's critique of Aristotelian syllogistic. The passage you will read explains that the critique targets the demonstrative syllogism (al-qiyas al-burhani) and its adjuncts, complete induction and analogy. Crucially, the critique has two sides: a destructive side that refutes Aristotelian forms and a constructive side that proposes Islamic syllogistic forms. Understanding this dual structure is key to appreciating Ibn Taymiyya's contribution to Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target of Ibn Taymiyya's critique
The passage states that the critique targets 'the Aristotelian theory of syllogisms in its final form—namely, the demonstrative syllogism.' Locate the Arabic terms used for this target.
Look for 'القياس البرهاني' (al-qiyas al-burhani).
2
Recognize the formal emphasis
The author says the critique is 'from the formal perspective' (من الناحية الصورية) even though it occasionally touches material aspects. Determine why 'formal' is highlighted here.
Formal here refers to the logical structure, not the content of premises.
3
Differentiate destructive and constructive aspects
The passage divides the critique into 'الجانب الهدمي' (destructive) and 'الجانب الإنشائي' (constructive). State what each aspect does according to the text.
Destructive refutes Aristotelian forms; constructive proposes Islamic alternative.
4
Connect to adjuncts: complete induction and analogy
The passage mentions 'complete induction' (الاستقراء الكامل) and 'analogy' (التمثيل) as appendages to the syllogism. Consider why Ibn Taymiyya would attack these as part of his formal critique.
These are alternative inferential methods that compete with Aristotelian demonstration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفَصْلُ الرَّابِع نقد نظرية الاستدلالات الارسططاليسية عند ابن تيمية بحثنا في الفصل نقد ابن تيمية لنظرية القضية عند أرسطو . والقضية الكلية اليقينية تمثل مادة البرهان ـ والبرهان بحث في الحق المطلق عند أرسطو يصل به إلى اليقين . وبهذا كان نقد ابن تيمية للقضية الكلية اليقينية إنما هو أيضاً نقد لنظرية الإستدلالات الأرسططاليسية في صورتها النهائية ـ وهي القياس البرهاني ـ أي القياس ذو المقدمات اليقينية . وقد فعل هذا المفكر الممتاز هذا ببراعة نادرة ، فهاجم مادة القضية الكلية اليقينية ، وبالتالي هاجم القياس . وقد أداه هذا إلى البحث في مادة القضية وبالتالي مادة القياس من نواحي منطقية وأبستمولوجية وميتافيزيقية وفيزيقية وإنسانية ودينية . فكان عليه بعد أن يهاجم نظرية الإستدلال في « صورتها » المنطقية وهذا ما فعله تماماً . وسنبحث في هذا الفصل نقد ابن تيمية من الناحية الصورية لمبحث القياس الأرسططاليسي ولواحقه من « استقراء كامل ، أو تمثيل » . وإن كان سيتعرض نقده أحياناً لنواح مادية ، غير أنه في جوهره صوري . ويتكون نقد ابن تيمية لمبحث الإستدلالات الأرسططاليسية من جانبين : جانب هدمي وجانب إنشائي . أما الجانب الأول فقد نقد فيه صور الإستدلالات الأرسططاليسية ، وأما الثاني فقد وضع فيه صور الإستدلالات الإسلامية أو التيمية .
Chapter Four: Critique of the Aristotelian Theory of Syllogisms According to Ibn Taymiyya. We discussed in the chapter Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the theory of the proposition according to Aristotle. The certain universal proposition represents the matter of demonstration—and demonstration investigates absolute truth according to Aristotle, leading to certainty. Hence Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the certain universal proposition is also a critique of the theory of Aristotelian syllogisms in their final form—namely, the demonstrative syllogism, i.e., the syllogism with certain premises. This excellent thinker did this with rare brilliance: he attacked the matter of the certain universal proposition, and thereby attacked the syllogism. This led him to investigate the matter of the proposition and consequently the matter of the syllogism from logical, epistemological, metaphysical, physical, human, and religious perspectives. So after attacking the theory of syllogism in its logical 'form', he did this completely. And we will examine in this chapter Ibn Taymiyya's critique from the formal perspective of the Aristotelian syllogism and its appendages: 'complete induction' or 'analogy'. Although his critique sometimes touches material aspects, in essence it is formal. Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the Aristotelian theory of syllogisms consists of two aspects: a destructive aspect and a constructive aspect. As for the first aspect, he criticized the forms of Aristotelian syllogisms, and as for the second, he established the forms of Islamic or Taymiyyan syllogisms.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage serves as the introduction to Chapter Four, laying out the plan for examining Ibn Taymiyya's critique of Aristotelian syllogistic. The author first reminds the reader that the previous chapter discussed Ibn Taymiyya's critique of Aristotelian proposition theory. Since the certain universal proposition is the matter (madda) of demonstration (burhan), and demonstration is the highest form of Aristotelian syllogism aimed at absolute truth, attacking the universal proposition inherently attacks demonstrative syllogism. Ibn Taymiyya thus investigated the proposition and syllogism from multiple angles: logical, epistemological, metaphysical, physical, human, and religious. The author clarifies that the present chapter focuses on the formal (suri) aspect of Ibn Taymiyya's critique concerning the syllogism itself and its adjuncts—complete induction (istiqra' kamil) and analogy (tamthil). Although material considerations appear occasionally, the core of the critique is formal. Ibn Taymiyya's critique has two sides: destructive (hadmi), in which he refutes Aristotelian syllogistic forms, and constructive (insha'i), in which he proposes alternative Islamic or 'Taymiyyan' syllogistic forms. This sets the stage for the detailed analysis to follow.

Key Terms
القياس البرهاني
الاستقراء الكامل
التمثيل
الجانب الهدمي
الجانب الإنشائي

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The formal aspect of the Aristotelian syllogism and its adjuncts.

D) The political and social dimensions of logic.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's critique of Aristotelian syllogistic is both destructive (refuting formal demonstrative syllogism) and constructive (proposing Islamic alternative syllogisms), with a primary focus on formal logical structure.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
Al-Suyuti's Two Scholarly Contributions in Logic Criticism
Author explains the historical arrangement and summarization of lost works in Suyuti's book.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify al-Suyuti's historical arrangement method and his summarization of lost works, and explain why each feature gives his book scholarly value.

Core Idea
Al-Suyuti's historical ordering and preservation of lost books make his work indispensable for studying the history of logic in Islam.

In this passage, a modern scholar highlights two features of al-Suyuti's book that make it invaluable. The first is its strict historical arrangement, tracing the criticism of logic from its origins to Ibn Taymiyya. The second is its summarization of lost or inaccessible books, especially Ibn Taymiyya's major work. These features allow you to understand the Islamic debate on logic in chronological depth and access key primary sources that are otherwise unavailable.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first feature
Find the phrase 'الترتيب التاريخي'. Al-Suyuti organized his material chronologically, from the origin of logic to its entry into Islam and the views of later scholars.
This is explicitly called the first feature (الميزة الأولى).
2
Understand the rationale behind historical arrangement
Al-Suyuti explains in the quoted passage that a mujtahid must know existing scholarly opinions before introducing a new ruling, to avoid breaking consensus. Thus historical arrangement is methodologically required in an ijtihad-based work.
This rationale is provided to justify the chronological ordering.
3
Identify the second feature
Look for 'تلخيص بعض الكتب المفقودة'. Al-Suyuti summarized lost or inaccessible books, especially Ibn Taymiyya's 'Nasihat Ahl al-Iman'. He preserved the original wording and only cut irrelevant material.
The summary is titled 'Jahd al-Qariha fi Tajrid al-Nasihat'.
4
Appreciate the scholarly value
Both features make al-Suyuti's book a unique resource: it provides a chronological map of the debate and preserves otherwise lost texts, aiding historical and philosophical study.
Without these features, much of Ibn Taymiyya's critique would be inaccessible.

السبكي على تحريم المنطق إطلاقاً ، بل أتى برأي بين التحليل والتحريم فيحرمه هو والفلسفة على من لم ترسخ قواعد الشريعة في قلبه ، ويمتلىء جوفه من عظمة هذا النبي الكريم وشريعته ، ويحفظ القرآن العزيز وشيئاً A comprehensive book on the prohibition of logic in the manner of ijtihad and reasoning, comprehensive and exclusive. When I started that and it necessitated transmitting the texts of the imams on prohibiting speculation in the science of kalam because of the entailment between them, I called the book 'Sawn al-Mantiq wa al-Kalam 'an Fanni al-Mantiq wa al-Kalam'. We will not discuss the topics of the book; we have already presented most of its topics in this book of ours. But we will try to show two important features of al-Suyuti's book that give it an invaluable scholarly value. The first feature: the historical arrangement of the book's topics. Al-Suyuti adhered to historical arrangement in his book. He spoke first about the origin of the invention of logic, then the beginning of its entry into the religion of Islam, and the beginning of its compilation of principles, and the beginning of its spread among the later scholars, then he proceeded to present the statements of Muslim scholars in criticizing logic and prohibiting it, observing a historical order. Al-Suyuti explained his method in his statement: 'He mentioned those who explicitly condemned or prohibited logic from the imams of Islam. There is no doubt that the mujtahid is forbidden to introduce a saying that no one has said before, and to invent an opinion that no one has preceded. For this reason, among the conditions of ijtihad is knowledge of the statements of the scholars from the Companions and those after them, both consensus and difference, so as not to break consensus in what he chooses. So it is necessary to mention the statements of the scholars on this issue before presenting the evidence because the book is authored in the manner of ijtihad.' Then al-Suyuti begins to present the statements of Muslim scholars in condemning and criticizing logic, starting with al-Shafi'i and ending with Ibn Taymiyya, and he observed this order in his discussion of the science of kalam. The second feature: summarizing some lost books. Al-Suyuti summarized for us, during his presentation of the opinions and statements of scholars in criticizing logic and kalam, books of these scholars, some of which are lost and some are not at hand now. So al-Suyuti greatly benefited the historical philosophical study. Among the most important books he summarized for us is Ibn Taymiyya's book on criticizing logic. Al-Suyuti mentions that Ibn Taymiyya has two books, one small which he could not see, and another large one in twenty booklets. The name of this book is 'Nasihat Ahl al-Iman fi al-Radd 'ala Mantiq al-Yunan', and that the latter book is what he will summarize, and this summary is called 'Jahd al-Qariha fi Tajrid al-Nasihat'. Then al-Suyuti summarizes the book accurately and understandably, and says at its end: 'This is the end of what I summarized from Ibn Taymiyya's book; I have presented his wording in his own words mostly without alteration, and I deleted much from his book, for it was in twenty booklets. I did not delete anything important; I only deleted what was not related to the purpose, such as digressions, or refutations of theological matters or the like, or repetitions, or criticisms of the expressions of some logicians, and not relating to a general principle of the art or the like. So if anyone reads my book, this summary, he will easily benefit from the purpose more than what he would get from the original, for it is rugged and difficult to access.' As for the original from which al-Suyuti derived his summary, it was published in (1368 AH - 1949 CE). It was one of our important references in this book. 2 - The Second Section: As for the second section, it is the one that discussed the issue of prohibition. Its most important representative is an excellent Shafi'i jurist, Abdul Wahhab al-Subki (771 AH). Al-Subki did not agree to the absolute prohibition of logic; rather he came with an opinion between permission and prohibition: he prohibits it and philosophy for those whose heart is not firmly rooted in the rules of Sharia, and whose innermost being is filled with the greatness of this noble Prophet and his Sharia, and who has memorized the noble Qur'an and a very great amount of the hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him) in the manner of the hadith scholars, and who knows from the branches of fiqh what...
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage discusses two important features of al-Suyuti's book 'Sawn al-Mantiq wa al-Kalam' that give it exceptional scholarly value. The author begins by noting that al-Suyuti's book is a comprehensive work on the prohibition of logic written in the manner of ijtihad and reasoning. He states the title of the book and explains that he will not delve into the topics of the book since they have already been covered, but instead will highlight two key features. The first feature is the historical arrangement of the book's topics. Al-Suyuti adhered to a chronological order, starting with the origin of logic, its entry into Islam, its compilation of principles, its spread among later scholars, and then presenting the views of Muslim scholars on its critique and prohibition. The author quotes al-Suyuti's own explanation of his methodology: he states that a mujtahid must know the existing scholarly opinions before introducing a new ruling, so it was necessary to present the statements of scholars on the prohibition of logic before providing proofs, in order to avoid breaking consensus. Al-Suyuti thus begins with al-Shafi'i and ends with Ibn Taymiyya, maintaining this order also in his discussion of kalam. The second feature is the summarization of lost books. Through his presentation of scholarly opinions, al-Suyuti summarized books from those scholars, many of which are now lost or unavailable. This greatly benefits historical and philosophical study. The author highlights in particular al-Suyuti's summary of Ibn Taymiyya's work on criticizing logic. Al-Suyuti mentions two books by Ibn Taymiyya: a small one he could not see, and a large one in twenty booklets titled 'Nasihat Ahl al-Iman fi al-Radd 'ala Mantiq al-Yunan'. Al-Suyuti's summary is called 'Jahd al-Qariha fi Tajrid al-Nasihat', and he summarizes it accurately, preserving Ibn Taymiyya's wording mostly, deleting only digressions, repetitions, and refutations unrelated to the main purpose. The author notes that the original was published in 1368 AH/1949 CE and served as an important reference. The passage then introduces the second section of the original work (القسم الثاني), which discusses the issue of prohibition of logic. Its main representative is the Shafi'i jurist Abdul Wahhab al-Subki, who did not approve of an absolute prohibition but took a middle position: he prohibits logic and philosophy for those not deeply rooted in Sharia, who have not fully internalized the greatness of the Prophet and his law, and who have not extensively memorized the Quran and hadith. The passage ends mid-sentence describing the required knowledge of fiqh branches.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
