Al-Subki's Middle Path on Logic: Conditions for Engagement
Author explains al-Subki's middle position on the permissibility of studying Aristotelian logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify al-Subki's two conditions for studying Aristotelian logic and explain how his position reconciled the practice of Shafi'i scholars with Ibn al-Salah's fatwa.

Core Idea
Al-Subki's compromise: logic is permitted only for the unshakable scholar who keeps Islamic discourse pure.

In the debate over Aristotelian logic, Taj al-Din al-Subki sought a middle ground. He acknowledged the dangers of mixing philosophy with theology, yet recognized the value of logic when used properly. This lesson unpacks his two conditions and the reasoning behind his influential compromise.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
Read the Arabic passage and locate the two conditions al-Subki sets for a scholar to study logic. They are introduced after 'لكن بشرطين'.
The conditions are: 1) confidence that one's faith is unshakable, and 2) not mixing logicians' speech with Islamic scholars' speech.
2
Understand al-Subki's critique of mixing
Notice that al-Subki admits harm has come from mixing philosophy with theology, citing attacks on Ash'aris and Shafi'is, and criticizes Nasir al-Din al-Tusi.
Al-Subki groups Ibn Taymiyya with the critics of Ash'arism, showing the tensions.
3
Recognize the exceptional scholars
Al-Subki makes an exception for al-Ghazali and al-Razi because they were fully grounded in religion before engaging with philosophy.
He stresses that they reached a level where their faith was not endangered, and they are not to be blamed.
4
Grasp al-Subki's reconciliation strategy
Al-Subki tried to reconcile the practice of Shafi'i scholars who studied logic with the fatwa of Ibn al-Salah prohibiting it, by imposing strict conditions.
This compromise allowed logic to be studied for refutation, not as a foundation, and was widely cited in later texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيه يسمى فقيهاً مفتياً مشاراً إليه من أهل مذهبه إذا وقعت حادثة فقهية ، وأما من وصل إلى هذا المقام الديني وتسلح بكل هذا فله النظر فيها للرد على المناطقة والمتعلقة ولكن بشرطين : أحدهما ، أن يثق من نفسه بأنه وصل إلى درجة لا تزعزعها رياح الأباطيل وشبه الأضاليل وأهواء الملاحدة . والثاني ، ان لا يمزج كلامهم بكلام علماء الإسلام . ويعترف السبكي بأنه قد حصل ضرر عظيم على المسلمين بمزج كلام الفلسفة بكلام المتكلمين . وأدى الحال إلى طعن المشبهة والحشوية والمجسمة من رعاع الخلق في الأشاعرة والشوافع . وهو يقصد ابن تيمية . ويهاجم نصير الدين الطوسي وغيره من أتباعه ممن قاموا بالمزج أما أن حجة الإسلام الغزالي والإمام فخر الدين الرازي قد خاضوا في علوم الفلسفة ودونوها وخلطوها بكلام المتكلمين فهو يعترف به . ولكنه لا ينكر إن هذين إمامان جليلان عظيمان ، ولم يخض واحد منهما في هذه العلوم ، حتى صار مكتملاً في الدين وأصبحا مثلاً كبرى في معرفة علم الكلام على طريقة أهل السنة والجماعة . فمن وصل إلى مقامهما ، لا يلام عليه النظر في الكتب الفلسفية ولا يمنع ، بل هو مثاب مأجور . من هذا يتبين لنا علة هذا الموقف الذي وقفه السبكي . فقد حاول أن يوفق بين اشتغال بعض علماء الشافعية بالمنطق الارسطوطاليسي ، وبين فتوى ابن الصلاح، فأدلى بهذا الرأي المتوسط . وكان لهذا الرأي أثر كبير فيما بعد فرددته كتب المنطق بحيث نراه في جميع شروح السلم للأخضري . ( ٩٤١هـ ) واستناداً على هذا الموقف استمر بعض علماء المسلمين في الكتابة والتصنيف في المنطق ومع ذلك يمكننا أن نقول إن كثيرين من فقهاء المسلمين استمروا على عداوتهم للمنطق الارسطوطاليسي وعلى تلمس الوسائل لمحاربته بعد ابن السبكي .
In it, one is called a jurist, mufti, and reference among the people of his school if a juristic incident occurs. As for one who reaches this religious station and arms himself with all this, he may examine it to respond to the logicians and those who engage in it, but with two conditions: One, that he be confident in himself that he has reached a degree not shaken by the winds of falsehoods, the semblances of misguidance, and the desires of atheists. And two, that he not mix their speech with the speech of the scholars of Islam. And al-Subki admits that great harm has befallen Muslims by mixing the speech of philosophy with the speech of the theologians. And the situation led to the criticism of the Mushabbiha, Hashwiyya, and Mujassima from the rabble of creation against the Ash'arites and Shafi'ites. And he means Ibn Taymiyya. And he attacks Nasir al-Din al-Tusi and others of his followers who carried out the mixing. As for Hujjat al-Islam al-Ghazali and Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi having delved into the sciences of philosophy and recorded them and mixed them with the speech of the theologians, he admits it. But he does not deny that these two are great, magnificent imams, and neither of them delved into these sciences until he became complete in religion and became great examples in knowledge of theology on the method of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a. So whoever reaches their station is not blamed for looking into philosophical books nor prevented; rather, he is rewarded and recompensed. From this, the reason for this stance that al-Subki took becomes clear to us. For he tried to reconcile the preoccupation of some Shafi'i scholars with Aristotelian logic and the fatwa of Ibn al-Salah, so he gave this intermediate opinion. And this opinion had a great impact afterwards, so that logic books repeated it such that we see it in all commentaries on al-Sullam by al-Akhdari (941 AH). And based on this stance, some Muslim scholars continued writing and authoring on logic, yet we can say that many Muslim jurists continued their enmity towards Aristotelian logic and seeking means to combat it after Ibn al-Subki.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage presents the historical context of the debate over studying Aristotelian logic within the Islamic scholarly tradition, focusing on the position of Taj al-Din al-Subki. The author begins by describing the high station of a jurist who is qualified to engage with logic: he must be a recognized mufti and authority in his school. Even then, engaging with logic is permitted only for refuting the logicians and only under two strict conditions: first, that the scholar's faith is unshakable by falsehoods and doubts; second, that he never mixes the logicians' terminology with that of Islamic scholars. Al-Subki acknowledges the harm caused by earlier mixing of philosophy with theology, which led to attacks on Ash'aris and Shafi'is by groups like the Mushabbiha and Hashwiyya, targeting Ibn Taymiyya in particular. He criticizes Nasir al-Din al-Tusi for such mixing. However, al-Subki makes an exception for al-Ghazali and Fakhr al-Din al-Razi: they were already fully grounded in religious sciences before engaging with philosophy, so their work is praiseworthy. The key point is that only those who have reached the same level of religious mastery as those two imams may study philosophical books without blame. The author then explains that al-Subki's intermediate position was an attempt to reconcile the practice of Shafi'i logicians with the anti-logic fatwa of Ibn al-Salah. This compromise had lasting influence, appearing in later commentaries on al-Sullam. Despite this, many jurists remained hostile to logic after al-Subki.

المتعلقة

Key Takeaway
Al-Subki's compromise allowed elite scholars to study Aristotelian logic for the purpose of refutation, but only under strict conditions designed to protect Islamic discourse from corruption.

The Fuqaha and Aristotelian Logic: A Historical Debate
Historical overview of opposition to logic among fuqaha

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the main arguments for and against Aristotelian logic among Islamic jurists and explain how Tashkubri Zada defended it.

Core Idea
Logic was controversial among fuqaha, but defenders like Tashkubri Zada argued it is compatible with shari'a.

This lesson explores the historical debate over Aristotelian logic in Islamic scholarship. You will learn why some fuqaha rejected logic as foreign, how Tashkubri Zada countered those objections, and how the debate shaped later attitudes. Understanding this context helps you appreciate the diverse approaches to logic in the Islamic tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opposing positions
Find the main argument of the fuqaha who opposed logic: they considered it non-shari'i because it is not found in the Prophetic heritage and thus unnecessary for Islamic sciences.
2
Extract Tashkubri Zada's three rebuttals
Notice how Tashkubri Zada addresses three possible meanings of 'non-shari'i': (1) contradictory to shari'a (ignorance), (2) form not taken from shari'a (false, as burhan is in Quran and Hadith), (3) invented by philosophers (not a problem, like arithmetic).
This is the core analytical move of the passage.
3
Note the defense of logic's value
Observe Tashkubri Zada's praise of logic as chief of sciences, even fard al-ayn for knowing the absolute obligatory, and its role in Sufi practice.
This shows the high esteem some scholars had for logic.
4
Trace the historical trajectory
Follow the summary of criticism from al-Ghazali's integration, Ibn al-Salah's fatwa, Ibn Taymiyya's principled critique, and the later split into two schools.
This gives you a map of the debate's development.
5
Evaluate the debate's significance
Consider why this matters: the fuqaha's engagement with logic shaped how Islamic sciences used rational methods, and understanding this helps you contextualize later logical works.
Think about how an awareness of this debate affects a modern student of logic.

إختلاف الفقهاء في اعتبار المنطق علمياً شرعياً أو غير شرعي. ويذكر أن الفقهاء أخرجوه من نطاق المباحث الشرعية، ما دام هذا العلم غير موجود في التراث الإسلامي النبوي. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك فلا حاجة للعلوم الشرعية إليه، لأن العلوم الشرعية لا تحتاج إلى علم ليس فيها. وينكر طاش كبري زادة هذا أشد الإنكار، ويرى أن من قال إن المنطق علم غير شرعي، إن أراد بذلك ما يخالف الشرع فهذا من عدم الوقوف على المسائل المنطقية وجهله بها وإن أراد أن صورته غير مأخوذة من الشرع فليس كذلك. إذ كم من أقيسة برهانية واردة في الشرع ومبثوثة فيه. وإن أراد أنه مما وضعته الفلاسفة مادة، فليس في ذلك نقض، بعد أن لم يخالف الشرع، وهناك علوم مثل علم الحساب الذي وضعته الفلاسفة، له مدخل عظيم وتطبيق في كثير عن الأمور الشرعية ولم يقدح فيه واحد من علماء الشريعة. ثم يتكلم طاش كبري زادة عن فضل المنطق على العلوم ويقول « إن بعض الحكماء أجراه مجرى الرئيس للعلوم العقلية، حتى إن بعض العلماء جعلوه من فروض العين لكونه موقوفاً عليه الواجب المطلق. وكمان مقدوراً للمكلف حتى حكم بعض من الحكماء الاشراقية أن رياضة المتصوفة وسلوكهم تدور أيضاً على قواعد المنطق وبالجملة المنطق علم باهر البرهان، وكالشمس لا تخفى بكل مكان، ولا يجحد فضله إلا من يعشو عن إدراك الحقائق، ويعمى عن فهم الدقائق » ثم يورد شعراً في مدح المنطق وشعراً في ذمه. هذا الدفاع الشديد عن المنطق يثبت تماماً أنه كانت هناك معارضة قوية له بين طبقات الفقهاء في العصر الذي عاش فيه طاش كبري زادة. ويرى حاجي خليفة ( ١٠٦٧هـ ـ ١٦٥٨م ) ـ وهو مؤرخ متأخر ولعله يؤرخ أيضاً لعصره ـ أن الفقهاء يهاجمون المنطق الارسططاليسي وهو أصل كل علم وتقويم كل ذهن، ولا يخرج تحليله لمهاجمة المنطق الارسططاليسي والدفاع عنه عن كلام طاش كبري زادة. من كل هذا نستنتج أن بعض الفقهاء استمروا على عداوتهم للمنطق الارسططاليسي إلى وقت متأخر من تاريخ الإسلام. ولكننا لا نجد في هذا العصر المتأخر محاولات نقدية تشبه المحاولات التي قام بها ابن تيمية والتي نقد بها المنطق الارسططاليسي في أصوله. فالفقهاء إذن قاموا بالهجوم على المنطق الارسططاليسي ونقده. رأينا هذا الهجوم أولاً على المنطق الارسططاليسي في موقف علماء المسلمين تجاه الغزالي، وهو أول من مزج منطق أرسطو بعلوم المسلمين. ثم رأيناه في محاربة علماء المشرق والمغرب لهذا المنطق والمشتغلين به. وعرضنا كيف ظهر النقد عند ابن الصلاح في صورة فتوى تحرم الاشتغال به. ثم توجه نقد المنطق الارسططاليسي وجهة أخرى على يد ابن تيمية. فنقد هذا المنطق نقداً علمياً، وحاول أن يضع آراء جديدة في المنطق. وانتهى النقد في مدرسة الفقهاء بعد ابن تيمية إلى فريقين من العلماء ـ كما قلنا ـ فريق تابع ابن تيمية في نقده لأصول منطق أرسطو، وفريق تابع ابن الصلاح في البحث في مسألة التحريم.
The disagreement of the fuqaha regarding considering logic a shari'i science or non-shari'i. And it is mentioned that the fuqaha excluded it from the scope of shari'i investigations, as long as this science does not exist in the prophetic Islamic heritage. And if the matter is so, then there is no need for the shari'i sciences to it, because the shari'i sciences do not need a science that is not in them. And Tashkubri Zada denies this most vehemently, and he holds that whoever says that logic is a non-shari'i science—if he means by that what contradicts the shari'a, then this is from lack of familiarity with logical issues and ignorance of them; and if he means that its form is not taken from the shari'a, then it is not so. For how many demonstrative syllogisms are present in the shari'a and scattered in it. And if he means that it is something that the philosophers invented as matter, then there is no objection in that, after it does not contradict the shari'a, and there are sciences like the science of arithmetic which the philosophers invented, which has a great entry and application in many shari'i matters, and not one of the scholars of the shari'a has criticized it. Then Tashkubri Zada speaks about the superiority of logic over the sciences and says: 'Indeed some of the sages made it like the chief of the rational sciences, to the extent that some scholars made it from the fard al-ayn because the absolute obligatory depends upon it. And it is a means for the responsible person, so that some of the ishraqi sages judged that the spiritual exercise of the Sufis and their conduct also revolve around the rules of logic. In summary, logic is a science brilliant in proof, like the sun not hidden in any place, and its superiority is denied only by one who is blind to the perception of truths and blind to understanding subtleties.' Then he cites poetry in praise of logic and poetry in dispraise of it. This strong defense of logic proves completely that there was strong opposition to it among the classes of fuqaha in the era in which Tashkubri Zada lived. And Haji Khalifa (1067 AH - 1658 CE)—who is a later historian and perhaps also writes about his own era—holds that the fuqaha attack Aristotelian logic, which is the origin of every science and the straightening of every mind, and his analysis of attacking Aristotelian logic and defending it does not go beyond the words of Tashkubri Zada. From all this we conclude that some fuqaha continued their enmity towards Aristotelian logic until a late time in Islamic history. But we do not find in this later era critical attempts that resemble the attempts made by Ibn Taymiyya, by which he criticized Aristotelian logic in its principles. So the fuqaha indeed attacked Aristotelian logic and criticized it. We saw this attack first on Aristotelian logic in the stance of Muslim scholars towards al-Ghazali, who was the first to mix Aristotle's logic with Islamic sciences. Then we saw it in the struggle of the scholars of the East and the West against this logic and those engaged in it. And we presented how criticism appeared at Ibn al-Salah in the form of a fatwa prohibiting occupation with it. Then the criticism of Aristotelian logic took another direction at the hands of Ibn Taymiyya. For he criticized this logic scientifically, and attempted to put forward new views in logic. And the criticism in the school of the fuqaha after Ibn Taymiyya ended with two groups of scholars—as we said—a group following Ibn Taymiyya in his criticism of the principles of Aristotle's logic, and a group following Ibn al-Salah in researching the issue of prohibition.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage is a historical overview of the opposition to Aristotelian logic among Islamic jurists (fuqaha). It begins by noting the disagreement among fuqaha over whether logic is a legitimate shari'i science. Some fuqaha argued that since logic is not found in the Prophetic tradition, it is unnecessary for the shari'i sciences. The author then introduces the prominent Ottoman scholar Tashkubri Zada, who vehemently refutes this position. Tashkubri Zada argues that if someone claims logic contradicts the shari'a, it is due to ignorance; if they claim its form is not derived from the shari'a, they are wrong because many demonstrative syllogisms are found in the Quran and Hadith. If they claim it was invented by the Greek philosophers, he points out that other sciences like arithmetic, also invented by philosophers, are used in shari'a matters without objection. Tashkubri Zada then extols logic as the chief of rational sciences, with some scholars considering it a personal obligation (fard al-ayn) because it is necessary for knowing the absolute obligatory (i.e., knowledge of God). He even quotes ishraqi sages who say Sufi spiritual exercises are based on logic. The author notes that Tashkubri Zada's strong defense indicates fierce opposition to logic among fuqaha of his time. Next, the author references the later historian Haji Khalifa, who confirms that fuqaha attacked Aristotelian logic despite its being foundational. The passage concludes by summarizing the trajectory of criticism: beginning with al-Ghazali's integration of logic, followed by Ibn al-Salah's fatwa prohibiting it, and then the more systematic critique of Ibn Taymiyya. After Ibn Taymiyya, the fuqaha split into two camps: those who followed Ibn Taymiyya's principled critique and those who followed Ibn al-Salah's prohibition approach. All examples in the commentary must be from Islamic sciences: for instance, the use of arithmetic in calculating inheritance (fara'id) is a shari'i application of a non-shari'i science.

المنطق
فروض العين
برهان

Checkpoint
According to Tashkubri Zada, what is one reason logic should not be rejected even if it originated from philosophers?
A) Logic was revealed to the Prophet Muhammad.
B) Other sciences like arithmetic, which also originated from philosophers, are accepted and used in shari'a matters.
C) Logic is explicitly mentioned in the Quran.
D) The jurists all agree that logic is obligatory.

Key Takeaway
The debate over Aristotelian logic among fuqaha ranged from outright rejection to conditional acceptance, with Tashkubri Zada defending logic by comparing it to arithmetic and citing its presence in Islamic sources.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Compare Tashkubri Zada's three arguments with Ibn Taymiyya's critique. Identify which of Tashkubri Zada's points Ibn Taymiyya would accept and which he would challenge.
?
Research a specific example of a demonstrative syllogism (burhan) in the Quran or Hadith that Tashkubri Zada could have used. Present it and explain its logical structure.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
The Three Phases of Juristic Response to Aristotelian Logic
Author summarizes historical attitudes of Muslim jurists towards Aristotelian logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three main phases of juristic response to Aristotelian logic as described by the author.

Core Idea
Juristic responses evolved from opposition to al-Ghazali, to prohibition by Ibn al-Salah, to scientific critique by Ibn Taymiyya.

This passage surveys how Muslim jurists engaged with Aristotelian logic over centuries. The author identifies a sequence of responses: initial resistance to al-Ghazali's integration, a fatwa of prohibition from Ibn al-Salah, and finally a deep scientific critique by Ibn Taymiyya. Understanding this timeline helps you see why later debates about logic took the form they did.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three key figures mentioned
Scan the Arabic for al-Ghazali, Ibn al-Salah, and Ibn Taymiyya. Note the order in which they appear.
They appear in chronological order.
2
Determine each figure's role
What did each do? Al-Ghazali mixed logic with Islamic sciences; Ibn al-Salah prohibited it; Ibn Taymiyya critiqued its foundations scientifically.
3
Map the sequence of events
Create a mental timeline: first response to al-Ghazali, then Ibn al-Salah's fatwa, then Ibn Taymiyya's critique.
The author presents this development.
4
Contrast the nature of each response
Compare the type of response: reactionary opposition, legal prohibition, and scientific refutation.
Ibn Taymiyya's is the most radical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
علم وتقويم كل ذهن ، ولا يخرج تحليله لمهاجمة المنطق الارسططاليسي والدفاع عنه عن كلام طاش كبري زادة(١) . من كل هذا نستنتج أن بعض الفقهاء استمروا على عداوتهم للمنطق الارسططاليسي إلى وقت متأخر من تاريخ الإسلام . ولكننا لا نجد في هذا العصر المتأخر محاولات نقدية تشبه المحاولات التي قام بها ابن تيمية والتي نقد بها المنطق الارسططاليسي في أصوله . فالفقهاء إذن قاموا بالهجوم على المنطق الارسططاليسي ونقده . رأينا هذا الهجوم أولاً على المنطق الارسططاليسي في موقف علماء المسلمين تجاه الغزالي ، وهو أول من مزج منطق أرسطو بعلوم المسلمين . ثم رأيناه في محاربة علماء المشرق والمغرب لهذا المنطق والمشتغلين به . وعرضنا كيف ظهر النقد عند ابن الصلاح في صورة فتوى تحرم الاشتغال به . ثم توجه نقد المنطق الارسططاليسي وجهة أخرى على يد ابن تيمية . فنقد هذا المنطق نقداً علمياً ، وحاول أن يضع آراء جديدة في المنطق . وانتهى النقد في مدرسة الفقهاء بعد ابن تيمية إلى فريقين من العلماء ـ كما قلنا ـ فريق تابع ابن تيمية في نقده لأصول منطق أرسطو ، وفريق تابع ابن الصلاح في البحث في مسألة التحريم . (١) حاجي خليفة . كشف الظنون ( ليبزج ) ص ١١١ - جـ ١ .
The discipline and correction of every mind, and its analysis for attacking Aristotelian logic and defending it does not depart from the words of Tashkubri Zada (1). From all this we conclude that some jurists continued their hostility towards Aristotelian logic until a late time in Islamic history. But we do not find in this late period critical attempts resembling those undertaken by Ibn Taymiyya, with which he criticized Aristotelian logic in its principles. Thus the jurists attacked Aristotelian logic and criticized it. We saw this attack first on Aristotelian logic in the stance of Muslim scholars towards al-Ghazali, who was the first to mix Aristotle's logic with the Islamic sciences. Then we saw it in the struggle of the scholars of the East and the West against this logic and those who engaged in it. And we presented how criticism appeared from Ibn al-Salah in the form of a fatwa prohibiting engaging with it. Then the criticism of Aristotelian logic turned in another direction at the hands of Ibn Taymiyya. For he criticized this logic scientifically, and attempted to set forth new views in logic. And the criticism in the school of jurists after Ibn Taymiyya ended with two groups of scholars — as we said — a group following Ibn Taymiyya in his criticism of the principles of Aristotle's logic, and a group following Ibn al-Salah in investigating the issue of prohibition. (1) Hajji Khalifa, Kashf al-Zunun (Leipzig), p. 111, vol. 1.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage is a historical overview by the author of the attitudes of Muslim jurists (fuqaha') towards Aristotelian logic. The author begins by noting that the analysis of attacking or defending Aristotelian logic is not new but is already found in the work of Tashkubri Zada (a 16th-century Ottoman scholar). He then concludes that some jurists maintained hostility to Aristotelian logic into late Islamic history, but that later criticism never matched the depth of Ibn Taymiyya's critique. The author traces the development: first, the opposition to al-Ghazali, who was the first to integrate Aristotelian logic into Islamic sciences; then, the resistance from scholars of the East and West against both the logic and its practitioners; then Ibn al-Salah's fatwa prohibiting its study; finally, Ibn Taymiyya's scientific critique, which went beyond prohibition to a systematic rejection of its foundations. After Ibn Taymiyya, the jurists split into two groups: those who followed Ibn Taymiyya in criticizing the principles of Aristotelian logic, and those who followed Ibn al-Salah and focused on the question of its legal prohibition (tahrim). The passage is prefatory, setting the stage for the author's own contribution.

Key Terms
المنطق الارسططاليسي (al-mantiq al-aristatalisi): Aristotelian logic; the system of logic derived from Aristotle's Organon, which Muslim scholars engaged with and critiqued. [Note: In this text, it refers specifically to the Greek logical tradition as opposed to later developments in Islamic logic.]
ابن تيمية (Ibn Taymiyya): The Hanbali theologian and jurist (d. 1328) who wrote a comprehensive critique of Aristotelian logic in his work al-Radd 'ala al-Mantiqiyyin. [Note: Ibn Taymiyya's critique is more radical than earlier juristic opposition; he attacks the very foundations of Aristotelian logic.]
ابن الصلاح (Ibn al-Salah): The Shafi'i hadith scholar (d. 1245) who issued a fatwa prohibiting the study of logic. [Note: Ibn al-Salah's position is representative of the earlier juristic opposition that focused on prohibition rather than scientific refutation.]
الغزالي (al-Ghazali): The Ash'ari theologian and Sufi (d. 1111) who integrated Aristotelian logic into Islamic epistemology in works like Mi'yar al-'Ilm. [Note: Al-Ghazali is credited as the first to 'mix' Aristotelian logic with Islamic sciences, sparking both adoption and opposition.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which figure is associated with a scientific critique of the principles of Aristotelian logic?
A) al-Ghazali
B) Ibn al-Salah
C) Ibn Taymiyya
D) Tashkubri Zada

Key Takeaway
Muslim jurists' responses to Aristotelian logic evolved from opposition to al-Ghazali, to prohibition by Ibn al-Salah, to scientific critique by Ibn Taymiyya.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
List the three phases and for each, write one sentence describing the main figure and their contribution.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
Ibn Taymiyya's Scientific Critique as a Turning Point
Author summarizes historical attitudes of Muslim jurists towards Aristotelian logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe how Ibn Taymiyya's critique differed from earlier juristic opposition to Aristotelian logic.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya shifted the debate from prohibition to a fundamental critique of logical principles.

The passage tells us that Ibn Taymiyya's critique was 'scientific' and attempted to 'set forth new views in logic.' Unlike Ibn al-Salah's fatwa, which simply forbade the study, Ibn Taymiyya engaged with the very principles (usul) of Aristotelian logic. This lesson focuses on what made his approach unique.

Guided Path
1
Find how the author contrasts Ibn Taymiyya with earlier critics
Look for phrases like 'scientific critique' and 'new views'. The author says later criticism didn't match Ibn Taymiyya's depth.
2
Identify the target of Ibn Taymiyya's critique
He criticized Aristotelian logic 'in its principles' (usul). That means he went beyond rejecting the study to attacking the theoretical foundations.
This is a deeper level of engagement.
3
Compare with Ibn al-Salah's approach
Ibn al-Salah issued a fatwa (legal prohibition). Ibn Taymiyya wrote a systematic refutation (al-Radd 'ala al-Mantiqiyyin).
One is legal, the other is epistemological.

علم وتقويم كل ذهن ، ولا يخرج تحليله لمهاجمة المنطق الارسططاليسي والدفاع عنه عن كلام طاش كبري زادة(١) . من كل هذا نستنتج أن بعض الفقهاء استمروا على عداوتهم للمنطق الارسططاليسي إلى وقت متأخر من تاريخ الإسلام . ولكننا لا نجد في هذا العصر المتأخر محاولات نقدية تشبه المحاولات التي قام بها ابن تيمية والتي نقد بها المنطق الارسططاليسي في أصوله . فالفقهاء إذن قاموا بالهجوم على المنطق الارسططاليسي ونقده . رأينا هذا الهجوم أولاً على المنطق الارسططاليسي في موقف علماء المسلمين تجاه الغزالي ، وهو أول من مزج منطق أرسطو بعلوم المسلمين . ثم رأيناه في محاربة علماء المشرق والمغرب لهذا المنطق والمشتغلين به . وعرضنا كيف ظهر النقد عند ابن الصلاح في صورة فتوى تحرم الاشتغال به . ثم توجه نقد المنطق الارسططاليسي وجهة أخرى على يد ابن تيمية . فنقد هذا المنطق نقداً علمياً ، وحاول أن يضع آراء جديدة في المنطق . وانتهى النقد في مدرسة الفقهاء بعد ابن تيمية إلى فريقين من العلماء ـ كما قلنا ـ فريق تابع ابن تيمية في نقده لأصول منطق أرسطو ، وفريق تابع ابن الصلاح في البحث في مسألة التحريم . The discipline and correction of every mind, and its analysis for attacking Aristotelian logic and defending it does not depart from the words of Tashkubri Zada (1). From all this we conclude that some jurists continued their hostility towards Aristotelian logic until a late time in Islamic history. But we do not find in this late period critical attempts resembling those undertaken by Ibn Taymiyya, with which he criticized Aristotelian logic in its principles. Thus the jurists attacked Aristotelian logic and criticized it. We saw this attack first on Aristotelian logic in the stance of Muslim scholars towards al-Ghazali, who was the first to mix Aristotle's logic with the Islamic sciences. Then we saw it in the struggle of the scholars of the East and the West against this logic and those who engaged in it. And we presented how criticism appeared from Ibn al-Salah in the form of a fatwa prohibiting engaging with it. Then the criticism of Aristotelian logic turned in another direction at the hands of Ibn Taymiyya. For he criticized this logic scientifically, and attempted to set forth new views in logic. And the criticism in the school of jurists after Ibn Taymiyya ended with two groups of scholars — as we said — a group following Ibn Taymiyya in his criticism of the principles of Aristotle's logic, and a group following Ibn al-Salah in investigating the issue of prohibition. The passage is prefatory, setting the stage for the author's own contribution.

Key Terms
المنطق الارسططاليسي (al-mantiq al-aristatalisi): Aristotelian logic; the system of logic derived from Aristotle's Organon, which Muslim scholars engaged with and critiqued. [Note: In this text, it refers specifically to the Greek logical tradition as opposed to later developments in Islamic logic.]
ابن تيمية (Ibn Taymiyya): The Hanbali theologian and jurist (d. 1328) who wrote a comprehensive critique of Aristotelian logic in his work al-Radd 'ala al-Mantiqiyyin. [Note: Ibn Taymiyya's critique is more radical than earlier juristic opposition; he attacks the very foundations of Aristotelian logic.]
ابن الصلاح (Ibn al-Salah): The Shafi'i hadith scholar (d. 1245) who issued a fatwa prohibiting the study of logic. [Note: Ibn al-Salah's position is representative of the earlier juristic opposition that focused on prohibition rather than scientific refutation.]
الغزالي (al-Ghazali): The Ash'ari theologian and Sufi (d. 1111) who integrated Aristotelian logic into Islamic epistemology in works like Mi'yar al-'Ilm. [Note: Al-Ghazali is credited as the first to 'mix' Aristotelian logic with Islamic sciences, sparking both adoption and opposition.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what did Ibn Taymiyya attempt to do?
A) Issue a fatwa prohibiting logic
B) Mix logic with Islamic sciences
C) Criticize Aristotelian logic scientifically and set forth new views
D) Defend Aristotelian logic

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's critique was a turning point because it addressed the principles of logic itself, not just its permissibility.

The Split Among Jurists After Ibn Taymiyya
Author summarizes historical attitudes of Muslim jurists towards Aristotelian logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two groups of jurists that emerged after Ibn Taymiyya and their respective focuses.

Core Idea
After Ibn Taymiyya, jurists divided into followers of his scientific critique and followers of Ibn al-Salah's prohibition approach.

The passage concludes by noting a split: one group followed Ibn Taymiyya in critiquing the principles of logic, while another followed Ibn al-Salah by focusing on whether logic is legally prohibited. This division shaped later discussions.

Guided Path
1
Locate the description of the split in the Arabic
Find 'فريق تابع ابن تيمية' and 'فريق تابع ابن الصلاح'.
2
Characterize each group
One group engaged with logic at the principle level; the other was concerned with the legal ruling (tahrim).
These are different intellectual projects.
3
Consider why this split matters
It shows that after Ibn Taymiyya, there was no single juristic stance; both approaches coexisted.

The discipline and correction of every mind, and its analysis for attacking Aristotelian logic and defending it does not depart from the words of Tashkubri Zada (1). From all this we conclude that some jurists continued their hostility towards Aristotelian logic until a late time in Islamic history. But we do not find in this late period critical attempts resembling those undertaken by Ibn Taymiyya, with which he criticized Aristotelian logic in its principles. Thus the jurists attacked Aristotelian logic and criticized it. We saw this attack first on Aristotelian logic in the stance of Muslim scholars towards al-Ghazali, who was the first to mix Aristotle's logic with the Islamic sciences. Then we saw it in the struggle of the scholars of the East and the West against this logic and those who engaged in it. And we presented how criticism appeared from Ibn al-Salah in the form of a fatwa prohibiting engaging with it. Then the criticism of Aristotelian logic turned in another direction at the hands of Ibn Taymiyya. For he criticized this logic scientifically, and attempted to set forth new views in logic. And the criticism in the school of jurists after Ibn Taymiyya ended with two groups of scholars — as we said — a group following Ibn Taymiyya in his criticism of the principles of Aristotle's logic, and a group following Ibn al-Salah in investigating the issue of prohibition. (1) Hajji Khalifa, Kashf al-Zunun (Leipzig), p. 111, vol. 1.

Commentary
This passage is a historical overview by the author of the attitudes of Muslim jurists (fuqaha') towards Aristotelian logic. The author begins by noting that the analysis of attacking or defending Aristotelian logic is not new but is already found in the work of Tashkubri Zada (a 16th-century Ottoman scholar). He then concludes that some jurists maintained hostility to Aristotelian logic into late Islamic history, but that later criticism never matched the depth of Ibn Taymiyya's critique. The author traces the development: first, the opposition to al-Ghazali, who was the first to integrate Aristotelian logic into Islamic sciences; then, the resistance from scholars of the East and West against both the logic and its practitioners; then Ibn al-Salah's fatwa prohibiting its study; finally, Ibn Taymiyya's scientific critique, which went beyond prohibition to a systematic rejection of its foundations. After Ibn Taymiyya, the jurists split into two groups: those who followed Ibn Taymiyya in criticizing the principles of Aristotelian logic, and those who followed Ibn al-Salah and focused on the question of its legal prohibition (tahrim). The passage is prefatory, setting the stage for the author's own contribution.

ابن تيمية (Ibn Taymiyya): The Hanbali theologian and jurist (d. 1328) who wrote a comprehensive critique of Aristotelian logic in his work al-Radd 'ala al-Mantiqiyyin. [Note: Ibn Taymiyya's critique is more radical than earlier juristic opposition; he attacks the very foundations of Aristotelian logic.]
ابن الصلاح (Ibn al-Salah): The Shafi'i hadith scholar (d. 1245) who issued a fatwa prohibiting the study of logic. [Note: Ibn al-Salah's position is representative of the earlier juristic opposition that focused on prohibition rather than scientific refutation.]
الغزالي (al-Ghazali): The Ash'ari theologian and Sufi (d. 1111) who integrated Aristotelian logic into Islamic epistemology in works like Mi'yar al-'Ilm. [Note: Al-Ghazali is credited as the first to 'mix' Aristotelian logic with Islamic sciences, sparking both adoption and opposition.]

Learning Objective

Core Idea
The Illuminationists challenge the supremacy of discursive logic over intuitive knowledge.

This chapter heading marks a pivotal turn in the book 'Manahij Bahth' as it moves from discussing Peripatetic methods to evaluating the Illuminationist perspective. The Illuminationists, led by Suhrawardi, argue that theoretical research relies not only on syllogistic reasoning but also on direct intuition (shuhud). Understanding their position will help you appreciate the diversity of methodologies within Islamic philosophy.

Logical Method vs. Intuitive Method: Al-Ghazali's Classification
Author contrasts logical method with intuitive method, citing al-Ghazali's classification of truth seekers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the logical method (al-manhaj al-mantiqi) and the intuitive method (al-manhaj al-dhawqi) as presented by al-Ghazali, and identify which groups he associates with each method.

Core Idea
Al-Ghazali contrasts reason-based truth seekers with those who rely on direct spiritual taste.

This passage sets the stage for a comparison between two fundamentally different approaches to truth: the logical method of theologians and philosophers, and the intuitive method of the Sufis. The author traces the historical development of Muslim attitudes toward logic, culminating in al-Ghazali's attempt to integrate logic with Islamic sciences, which was met with resistance. The focus then shifts to al-Ghazali's own classification of truth seekers, where he critically evaluates each group and ultimately affirms only the Sufi path of direct experiential knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two methods
Locate in the Arabic where the author contrasts 'al-manhaj al-mantiqi' (logical method) and 'al-manhaj al-dhawqi' (intuitive method). Note the key terms: 'المنهج المنطقي' and 'المنهج الذوقي'. Understand that the logical method is associated with reason ('aql) and the intuitive method with taste (dhawq) and spiritual experience.
2
Recognize al-Ghazali's four groups
Find the passage where al-Ghazali lists the four types of truth seekers: theologians (mutakallimun), Batiniyyah (esotericists), philosophers (falasifa), and Sufis. Read the Arabic description for each: theologians claim 'ahl al-ra'y wa al-nazar', philosophers claim 'ahl al-mantiq wa al-burhan', Batiniyyah claim learning from an infallible imam, and Sufis claim 'ahl al-mushahada wa al-mukashafa'.
Note that the philosophers are explicitly linked to logic and demonstration.
3
Note al-Ghazali's rejection of three groups
Identify al-Ghazali's criticisms: theologians rely on premises from opponents and focus on contradictions; philosophy's logic can lead to contradictions when applied to Islamic sciences; the Batiniyyah's reliance on an infallible teacher is rejected. Understand that these rejections are based on their methods, not necessarily their conclusions.
Al-Ghazali initially endorsed logic but later critiqued its application.
4
Understand the epistemological basis of the Sufi method
Read the description of the Sufi method: it begins with Quranic absorption (istinbat qur'ani), moves to worship and spiritual states, and culminates in direct tasting (dhawq). Contrast this with the rational methods of the other groups. The passage emphasizes that this knowledge is attained not by learning but by direct experience.
The term 'dhawq' is a technical Sufi term for immediate experiential knowledge.

الفَصْلُ الأوّل
المنهج المنطقي والمنهج الذوقي
ذكرنا في الأبواب السابقة موقف علماء المسلمين من المنطق الأرسططاليسي ووصلنا إلى أن هؤلاء المسلمين لم يأخذوا بهذا المنطق من ناحية ، ووضعوا منطقاً آخر يخالفه من ناحية أخرى . واستمر موقفهم هذا حتى نهاية القرن الخامس . وفي نهاية هذا القرن مزج الغزالي المنطق بعلوم المسلمين . ولكن الفقهاء لم يوافقوا على هذه الحركة ، وقاوموها مقاومة شديدة . وسنحاول في هذا الفصل أن نبين منهج مفكرين آخرين عاشوا في العالم الإسلامي ولم يقبلوا شيئاً من صور التفكير المنطقية التي ذكرناها يونانية كانت أو إسلامية وهم الصوفية .
يتبين للباحث منذ بدء تكون الصوفية ، البون الشاسع بين منهجهم في المعرفة ، ومنهج المتكلمين والمناطقة ، فبينما كان مناهج هؤلاء الأخيرين يستند على العقل ، قدم الصوفية منهجاً جديداً ، أسموه أولاً ـ الاستنباط القرآني ، أن يردد الصوفي القرآن ، مستغرقاً فيه ، حتى تنفتح له المعاني الإلهية . ثم ينتقل إلى معاناة العبادة ، والخلوة والتردد بين المقامات الصوفية والأحوال حتى تنقدح المعرفة الإلهية فيه أو تلقى فيه ، فيتذوقها فإذا كان منهج المتكلمين والفلاسفة هو العقل ، فقد انتهى موقف الصوفية إلى الذوق والحدس ، ولسنا نخوض في هذين الطريقين ، إن البحث يكون أدخل في نطاق نظرية المعرفة الإبستمولوجيا ـ وإنما نبحث فيه من ناحية تأديته للصوفية
إلى رفض المنطق الأرسططاليسي من ناحية والمنطق الكلامي من ناحية أخرى

طريق الذوق .
The First Chapter: The Logical Method and the Intuitive Method. We mentioned in previous chapters the position of Muslim scholars on Aristotelian logic and concluded that these Muslims did not adopt this logic on one hand, and placed another logic that contradicted it on the other hand. Their position continued until the end of the fifth century. At the end of this century, al-Ghazali blended logic with the sciences of Muslims. But the jurists did not agree with this movement, and resisted it strongly. In this chapter we will try to show the method of other thinkers who lived in the Islamic world and did not accept any of the forms of logical thinking we mentioned, whether Greek or Islamic — they are the Sufis. It becomes clear to the researcher since the beginning of the formation of Sufism, the vast gap between their method of knowledge and the method of the theologians and logicians. While the methods of these latter were based on reason, the Sufis presented a new method, which they first called Quranic deduction: that the Sufi repeats the Quran, absorbed in it, until divine meanings open to him. Then he moves to the practice of worship, seclusion, and moving between Sufi stations and states until divine knowledge is ignited in him or cast into him, so he tastes it. If the method of the theologians and philosophers is reason, the position of the Sufis ended at taste and intuition. We do not delve into these two paths — the inquiry is more within the scope of epistemology — rather we investigate it from the aspect of its leading the Sufis to reject Aristotelian logic on one hand and theological logic on the other. The Sufis differed most severely with the philosophers and theologians regarding the nature of the method. They completely rejected reason and its foundations. Al-Ghazali draws for us in a wonderful picture the methods of the seekers of truth, and they are four: the theologians, who claim to be people of opinion and speculation; the Batiniyyah, who claim to be the people of instruction and designated for deriving from the infallible Imam; the philosophers, who claim to be the people of logic and demonstration; and the Sufis, who claim to be the elite of the Divine Presence and the people of witnessing and unveiling. (1) After that, al-Ghazali begins to deny the ways of the first three groups: the theologians, the Batiniyyah, and the philosophers. As for the theologians: their way in supporting their doctrines is that they relied on premises accepted from their opponents, and they were compelled to accept them either by imitation, consensus of the community, or mere acceptance from the Qur’an and reports. Most of their involvement was in extracting contradictions of opponents and holding them to the consequences of their accepted premises. This is of little benefit concerning what is not accepted as necessary at all. (2) We have seen before that al-Ghazali criticizes logicians for using weak proofs in research. Then al-Ghazali denies the way of philosophy — i.e., Aristotelian logic — and we have seen that after al-Ghazali recommended its use, he became aware of the contradictions that its application in Islamic sciences might produce, so he demolished his initial idea about it. Then al-Ghazali attacks the way of the Batiniyyah. (3) As for the only way to truth for him, it is what cannot be attained by learning but rather by taste, state, and change of attributes. It ends with two different ways of attaining knowledge: one is the way of speculation, and the other is the way of taste.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage serves as an introductory chapter that sets the stage for comparing the logical method with the intuitive method, specifically as represented by Sufism. The author begins by recapping the historical stance of Muslim scholars towards Aristotelian logic: initially they rejected it and developed their own logical system, but by the end of the fifth century AH, al-Ghazali attempted to integrate logic with Islamic sciences, a move that was met with strong resistance from jurists. The focus then shifts to a group that rejected all logical forms, both Greek and Islamic — the Sufis. The author highlights the fundamental epistemic divide: while theologians and philosophers rely on reason, the Sufis introduced a method initially called 'Quranic deduction' (al-istinbat al-qur'ani), where the Sufi immerses himself in the Qur'an until divine meanings are unveiled. This process evolves into worship, seclusion, and traversing spiritual stations, culminating in direct intuitive knowledge (dhawq). The author explicitly states that the method of theologians and philosophers is reason, whereas the Sufi endpoint is taste and intuition. He clarifies that his inquiry is epistemological, focusing on how the Sufi method led to the rejection of both Aristotelian and theological logic. The passage then quotes al-Ghazali's famous classification of truth seekers from his 'al-Munqidh min al-Dalal': theologians (ahl al-ra'y wa al-nazar), Batiniyyah (esotericists who claim learning from an infallible imam), philosophers (ahl al-mantiq wa al-burhan), and Sufis (ahl al-mushahada wa al-mukashafa). Al-Ghazali systematically refutes the first three groups. The theologians are criticized for basing their arguments on premises accepted from opponents, often through imitation, consensus, or mere textual acceptance, resulting in debates that focus on exposing contradictions rather than establishing truth. The philosophers' method (Aristotelian logic) is rejected because, despite al-Ghazali's earlier endorsement, he realized its application to Islamic sciences could lead to contradictions. The Batiniyyah's reliance on an infallible teacher is also attacked. Ultimately, al-Ghazali affirms only the Sufi path, which is attained not through learning but through direct experience (dhawq) and spiritual transformation. The passage concludes by noting that this results in two fundamentally different paths to knowledge: the way of speculation (nazar) and the way of taste (dhawq).

Key Terms
المنهج المنطقي
المنهج الذوقي
الذوق
الاستنباط القرآني
المشاهدة والمكاشفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Ghazali's classification in the passage, which group claims to be 'people of logic and demonstration'?
A) The theologians (mutakallimun)
B) The Batiniyyah (esotericists)
C) The philosophers (falasifa)
D) The Sufis

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali's classification illustrates a fundamental epistemic divide between rational methods (theologians, philosophers) and experiential methods (Sufis), with the latter alone leading to truth.

Parsing a Section Heading on Islamic Research Methodologies
Section heading introducing research methodologies in Islamic sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to parse a complex Arabic section heading and identify the three scientific domains it introduces.

Core Idea
Islamic scientific methodology spans chemistry, natural philosophy, and mathematics.

Section headings in classical Islamic works are not mere labels; they encode the author's organization of knowledge. This lesson walks you through unpacking such a heading, focusing on the key terms and their significance in Islamic intellectual history.

Guided Path
1
Identify the structural marker
The heading begins with 'البَابُ الخَامِسُ' (The Fifth Section). Notice that 'bāb' is a common structural division in Arabic scholarly works, often signaling a major thematic unit.
Different works use different terms: kitāb, bāb, faṣl, etc.
2
Parse the core phrase: 'مناهج البحث'
This phrase means 'research methodologies'. It tells us the section will discuss systematic approaches to inquiry. Note that 'manāhij' is the plural of 'minhāj', a well-known term in Islamic methodology.
Compare with 'manhaj' in uṣūl al-fiqh.
3
Identify the three scientific domains
The heading mentions three adjectives modifying 'al-ʿulūm' (sciences): الكيميائية (chemical), الطبيعية (natural/physical), الرياضية (mathematical). These correspond to three scientific fields that were highly developed in Islamic civilization.
Classical Islamic science classifications often include these as distinct categories.
4
Note the geographical/intellectual framework
The phrase 'في العالم الإسلامي' (in the Islamic world) situates the discussion historically and culturally, indicating that the author focuses on contributions from within Islamic civilization.
This is important for understanding the scope of the work.

The phrase 'in the Islamic world' (fī al-‘ālam al-islāmī) situates these methodologies within the historical and intellectual tradition of Islamic civilization. The heading serves to orient the reader and delimit the scope of the discussion that follows.

Identifying Authorial Purpose and Scope
Author explains scope of book on creative methodology of Muslims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish an author's stated purpose from other scholarly tasks, specifically between historical chronicle and methodological analysis.

Core Idea
The author's scope defines which questions the work answers.

Not every academic work aims to narrate history; some focus on extracting methods. This passage from a work on Muslim scientific methodology clarifies its own boundaries. Understanding the author's stated purpose is essential to correctly interpret the content and avoid misreading the work as a comprehensive historical account.

Guided Path
1
Identify what the author says he is NOT doing.
The author explicitly states: 'We are not attempting in this chapter to chronicle the transfer of chemical, natural, medical, and mathematical sciences to the Islamic world.' He postpones that for separate research.
This sets a negative boundary for the scope.
2
Identify what the author says he IS doing.
He says: 'we are chronicling the creative methodology of Muslims, and we do not address this transfer except in a general manner, only attempting to extract from it some models of methodology from some applied scientists.'
The positive scope is creative methodology, not history.
3
Notice how he positions his work relative to previous scholars.
He acknowledges the detailed works of Leclerc and Browne on the transfer of science, but differentiates his own focus. This shows he is building on their scholarship but with a different objective.
Recognizing this prevents confusion with prior works.
4
Recognize the implications for the reader.
The reader should expect a work that selects examples of methodology, not a chronological narrative. The author's clarity helps set expectations and allows the reader to assess the work's contribution accurately.
Understanding scope avoids misinterpretation of the book's value.

The First Chapter: The Transfer of Science to the Islamic World. We are not attempting in this chapter to chronicle the transfer of chemical, natural, medical, and mathematical sciences to the Islamic world. I am postponing this chronicle for a comprehensive independent research that I hope to undertake soon, God willing. Undoubtedly, the movement of transferring pre-Islamic scientific heritage - especially the Greek heritage - to the Islamic world has received attention from a generation of giant researchers, and they have attempted to clarify this transfer, its methods, approaches, and figures. Leclerc's work on 'History of Medicine among the Arabs' was a model to be emulated in its precision and comprehensiveness, and Browne also wrote on the same subject, then research continued in various domains and in other branches of science, natural, chemical, and mathematical. Despite the precision of what was written and the thorough knowledge of multiple researchers of their sources, the passage of time reveals to us new documents that enrich research and call for writing on it once, indeed many times. As for us in this book at hand, we are chronicling the creative methodology of Muslims, and we do not address this transfer except in a general manner, only attempting to extract from it some models of methodology from some applied scientists. Many historians of science have held that Arab science did not begin except when
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the first chapter of the book. The author clarifies that his purpose is not to provide a detailed chronicle of the transfer of scientific knowledge (chemical, natural, medical, mathematical) to the Islamic world. He mentions that he plans to do that separately. The author acknowledges the significant work of previous scholars like Leclerc and Browne who have studied this transfer, and notes that new documents continue to emerge. He then states the specific focus of the present book: to chronicle the creative methodology of Muslim scientists, and to only touch on the transfer in general terms in order to extract methodological examples from applied scientists. Thus, the passage sets the methodological and limited scope of the work, distinguishing it from purely historical accounts.

Three Migrations of Science to Baghdad
Author narrates the transfer of Greek and Indian sciences to Baghdad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three major migrations of scientific knowledge to the Islamic world as described in the passage.

Core Idea
Islamic science was built on Greek, Persian, and Indian foundations.

The author traces the journey of scientific knowledge from Alexandria to Baghdad, then details three waves of migration: Greek, Persian, and Indian. Understanding this transmission helps us see how Islamic science synthesized diverse traditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first migration
Find where the author describes the transfer of the Greek library from Alexandria to Baghdad. Note the cities: Antioch, Harran, Marv, then Baghdad.
This migration brought Greek medicine, philosophy, and sciences.
2
Identify the second migration
Locate the mention of a scientific migration from the lands of the non-Arabs (ancient Persia). This brought Persian science (علم إيران).
3
Identify the third migration
Find the migration from India and Sindh, carrying Indian medicine, astronomy, and mathematics.
These were detailed but unsystematic.
4
Summarize the three sources
List the three geographical sources and the type of knowledge each contributed. This sets the stage for understanding the unique Muslim contribution.
The author then introduces al-Biruni's comparative study.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
انتقل مجلس التعليم الطبي والعلمي من الاسكندرية في عهد الخليفة الأموي عمر بن عبد العزيز إلى انطاكية ومنها إلى حران ومنها إلى مرو متخذاً رحلة طويلة انتهت إلى بغداد ، ومن ثم بدأت المجامع العلمية في بغداد حركة الترجمة من ناحية ، والبحث العلمي من ناحية أخرى . وقد استدعى خلفاء بني العباس هؤلاء التراجمة إلى قصورهم ، وبني المأمون بعد ذلك بيت الحكمة ؛ وبدأ البحث التجريبي في مختلف العلوم .
نستطيع إذن أن نقول : إن تاريخ العلم عند العرب يبدأ بهجرة المكتبة اليونانية الاسكندرانية إلى بغداد ، ثم أعقبتها هجرة علمية أخرى من بلاد الأعاجم - فارس القديمة ، تحمل إلى العالم العربي - علم إيران - ثم هجرة ثالثة أتت من الهند والسند تحمل الكثير من آراء الهنود في الطب والفلك والرياضيات، وسنرى فيما بعد عالماً كبيراً هو البيروني يتكلم في كتابه «تحقيق ما للهند من مقولة » عن علوم الهند . وكان البيروني - فيما نعلم - عالماً ممحصاً وذا منهج مقارن ، وقد قام بمقارنة كل تلك العلوم الهندية التي وصل إلى معرفتها معرفة تكاد تكون تامة ، من طب وفلك ورياضيات بتراث اليونان العلمي ، ثم قارن كل هذا بما عند المسلمين . ولقد وصل البيروني إلى النتائج الخطيرة الآتية : أنه كان لدى الهنود علم جزئي كبير ، على درجة من التقدم ، لكن لا يربطه رباط علمي أو منهجي ، أبحاث متناثرة في الطب والرياضيات والفلكيات والطبيعيات، ولكن خالية من الإطار المنهجي الرائع . وكان لدى اليونان - على العكس من هذا - نظرية العلم ، نظرية البرهان . فبينما لا نجد لدى اليونان هذا العدد الجديد من أبحاث الهنود في مختلف العلوم الجزئية ، التي كان الهنود سادتها ، لا نجد لدى الهنود نظرية في العلم ، في البرهان دون المقدمات اليقينية ، قمة الفكر اليوناني . وسمة الحضارة اليونانية كلها . وكان الهنود يقفون منثالين متعجبين مسحورين ، حين يعرض لهم هذه النظرية الأخيرة ، بل إنهم نسبوه إلى السحر(١) . هذه النتائج الخطيرة التي توصل إليها هذا العالم المنهجي ، الذي كان له أثره الكبير في تطور العلم الإسلامي ، بمنهجه الإستقرائي الرائع كما كان له فضله العظيم في تاريخ العلوم عامة . ولكن البيروني لم يتنبه إلى فضله هو وفضل أسلافه من علماء التطبيق المسلمين إلى أن منهج المسلمين لم يكن هذا ولا ذاك . فلا هم شغلوا بالعلم الجزئي فحسب ، كما شغل الهنود ولم يشغلوا بالنظر ـ وآلته البرهان ، كما شغل اليونان ، وإنما توصلوا إلى المنهج الإستقرائي التجريبي كمنهج ، وطبقوه على علوم الهنود وعلوم اليونان .
على أية حال : كانت الفكرة الشائعة عن دخول العلم أنه أتى من خارج . وذهب مؤرخو العلم الإنساني إلى أن العرب تناولوا هذه العلوم بالأخذ أو بالتعديل أو بالنقد . وإذا كان مؤرخو العلم من الأوروبيين ، أنكروا فضل العرب الفلسفي ، فإنهم لم يستطيعوا على الإطلاق إنكار فضلهم العلمي ـ لكن على أساس أنه نتيجة لعلوم اليونان خاصة . ونحن نعلم أن أفكار الحسن بن الهيثم عاشت في أوروبا إلى زمان ليس ببعيد عنا ، كما نعلم أن أبحاث الطوسي في الرياضيات وتناوله لهندسة أقليدس ومصادراته ، بقيت زمناً طويلاً يتناولها علماء أوروبا ، كما نعلم وأن كتاب ابن سينا الطبي ـ القانون ، بقي المرجع الأساسي لكليات الطب في أوروبا حتى القرن السابع عشر ، وما زالت عناية الباحثين بالعلم العربي قائمة على أشدها ، يعنون بمكانته في التراث العلمي وقد وجه الأنظار إلى قيمة هذا العلم مؤرخ تاريخ العلم الإنساني : الأستاذ سارتون .
ولكن ينبغي أن نلاحظ أن السبب الأساسي في تطور العلم عند العرب ، أنهم قابلوه بروح جديدة في البحث كانت هي السبب الهام في تطوره ، ودفعه دفعة قوية إلى الأيام ، ولولا هذه « الدفعة » لما عاش ، ولما اعترف به مؤرخو العلم الأوروبيون أدنى اعتراف . لقد كان بيدهم المنهج الإستقرائي ، الذي اكتشفوه كما رأينا كاملاً . وقد رأينا حركة اكتشافه لدى مفكري الإسلام . وقد انتقل هذا المنهج إلى دوائر العلماء .
The medical and scientific educational council moved from Alexandria in the era of the Umayyad caliph Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz to Antioch, then to Harran, then to Marv, taking a long journey that ended in Baghdad. Then the scientific assemblies in Baghdad began the translation movement on one hand, and scientific research on the other. The Abbasid caliphs summoned these translators to their palaces, and al-Ma'mun later built the House of Wisdom; and experimental research began in various sciences.
So we can say: The history of science among the Arabs begins with the migration of the Alexandrian Greek library to Baghdad, then followed by another scientific migration from the lands of the non-Arabs – ancient Persia – carrying to the Arab world the science of Iran – then a third migration came from India and Sindh carrying many opinions of the Indians in medicine, astronomy, and mathematics. We will later see a great scholar, al-Biruni, speaking in his book "Taḥqīq mā li-l-Hind min maqūla" about the sciences of India. Al-Biruni – as far as we know – was a thorough scholar with a comparative methodology. He compared all those Indian sciences that he had come to know almost completely – medicine, astronomy, mathematics – with the Greek scientific heritage, then compared all this with what the Muslims had. Al-Biruni reached the following serious results: That the Indians had a great partial science, with a degree of advancement, but nothing binds it scientifically or methodologically – scattered researches in medicine, mathematics, astronomy, and physics – but devoid of the magnificent methodological framework. The Greeks – on the contrary – had the theory of science, the theory of demonstration. While we do not find among the Greeks this large number of Indian researches in various partial sciences, in which the Indians were masters, we do not find among the Indians a theory of science, of demonstration based on certain premises – the peak of Greek thought and the hallmark of the entire Greek civilization. The Indians stood amazed, astonished, spellbound, when this latter theory was presented to them; indeed they attributed it to magic. These serious results reached by this methodical scholar, who had a great impact on the development of Islamic science with his magnificent inductive method, and who had great merit in the history of sciences in general. But al-Biruni did not pay attention to his own merit and that of his predecessors among the practical Muslim scholars – that the method of the Muslims was not this nor that. They were not occupied solely with partial science, as the Indians were occupied, nor were they occupied with theory – and its instrument, demonstration – as the Greeks were occupied; rather they arrived at the empirical inductive method as a method, and applied it to the sciences of the Indians and the sciences of the Greeks.
In any case: The prevalent idea about the entry of science was that it came from outside. Historians of human science held that the Arabs took these sciences either by adoption, modification, or criticism. If the historians of science among the Europeans denied the philosophical merit of the Arabs, they were absolutely unable to deny their scientific merit – but on the basis that it is a result especially of the Greek sciences. We know that the ideas of al-Hasan ibn al-Haytham lived in Europe until a time not far from us, and that the researches of al-Tusi in mathematics and his handling of Euclid's geometry and his postulates remained for a long time dealt with by European scholars, and that Ibn Sina's medical book – al-Qānūn – remained the primary reference for medical faculties in Europe until the seventeenth century. The attention of researchers to Arabic science is still at its strongest, concerned with its place in the scientific heritage, and the historian of human science, Professor Sarton, has drawn attention to the value of this science.
But it should be noted that the basic reason for the development of science among the Arabs is that they met it with a new spirit in research, which was the important reason for its development and gave it a strong push forward. Without this 'push', it would not have lived, nor would European historians of science have accorded it any recognition. They had in their hands the inductive method, which they discovered completely as we have seen. And we have seen the movement of its discovery among the thinkers of Islam. And this method was transferred to the circles of scholars.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage is a historical introduction that traces the transmission of scientific knowledge from Greek, Persian, and Indian sources to the Islamic world, culminating in the development of a uniquely Islamic empirical inductive method. The author begins by describing the migration of the Alexandrian medical and scientific council through various cities to Baghdad, sparking translation and research. He then outlines three great migrations: the Greek library, Persian science, and Indian medicine/astronomy/mathematics. The focus then shifts to al-Biruni's comparative methodology: he compared Indian sciences (medicine, astronomy, mathematics) with Greek and Islamic sciences. Al-Biruni's key finding was that Indian sciences were extensive but lacked a systematic methodological framework, whereas Greek sciences had a theory of demonstration (burhān) but lacked the volume of empirical detail. The author then critiques al-Biruni for not recognizing that the Muslim method was neither purely empirical (like Indians) nor purely theoretical (like Greeks), but a synthesis: the empirical inductive method (al-manhaj al-istiqrā'ī al-tajrībī). The passage continues by discussing how European historians often attribute Arab scientific achievements to Greek influence, yet examples like Ibn al-Haytham's optics, al-Ṭūsī's mathematics, and Ibn Sīnā's Canon show lasting independent contributions. The author concludes that the essential reason for the advancement of Islamic science was the inductive method, which Muslims discovered and applied, giving science a powerful push that ensured its survival and recognition.

المنهج الاستقرائي
نظرية البرهان

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
5 min
Greek Theory vs. Indian Practice
Author contrasts Greek theory of demonstration with Indian empirical sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to contrast the Greek theory of demonstration (burhān) with the Indian partial sciences (ʿilm juzʾī) as presented by al-Biruni in the passage.

Core Idea
Greeks had theory but less detail; Indians had detail but no systematic method.

Al-Biruni's comparative analysis reveals a fundamental difference: Greek science possessed a rigorous theory of demonstration (burhān) but lacked the wealth of empirical observations found in Indian sciences. Indian sciences were extensive but unsystematic. This contrast sets up the need for a new method.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Greek contribution
Find where the passage describes Greek science as having 'naẓariyyat al-burhān' (theory of demonstration). Note that this is characterized as the peak of Greek thought and the hallmark of Greek civilization.
2
Identify the Indian contribution
Locate the description of Indian sciences as 'ʿilm juzʾī' (partial/empirical science) – vast but lacking a methodological framework. They were scattered researches in medicine, mathematics, astronomy, physics.
3
Contrast the two
The author states that Greeks had the theory but not the empirical abundance, while Indians had empirical detail but no systematic method. The Indians were amazed by the Greek theory, even calling it magic.
This contrast highlights that each tradition had something the other lacked.
4
Summarize al-Biruni's finding
Al-Biruni concluded that neither tradition alone was sufficient for comprehensive scientific progress. This motivates the search for a method combining both.

انتقل مجلس التعليم الطبي والعلمي من الاسكندرية في عهد الخليفة الأموي عمر بن عبد العزيز إلى انطاكية ومنها إلى حران ومنها إلى مرو متخذاً رحلة طويلة انتهت إلى بغداد ، ومن ثم بدأت المجامع العلمية في بغداد حركة الترجمة من ناحية ، والبحث العلمي من ناحية أخرى . وقد استدعى خلفاء بني العباس هؤلاء التراجمة إلى قصورهم ، وبني المأمون بعد ذلك بيت الحكمة ؛ وبدأ البحث التجريبي في مختلف العلوم .
نستطيع إذن أن نقول : إن تاريخ العلم عند العرب يبدأ بهجرة المكتبة اليونانية الاسكندرانية إلى بغداد ، ثم أعقبتها هجرة علمية أخرى من بلاد الأعاجم - فارس القديمة ، تحمل إلى العالم العربي - علم إيران - ثم هجرة ثالثة أتت من الهند والسند تحمل الكثير من آراء الهنود في الطب والفلك والرياضيات، وسنرى فيما بعد عالماً كبيراً هو البيروني يتكلم في كتابه «تحقيق ما للهند من مقولة » عن علوم الهند . وكان البيروني - فيما نعلم - عالماً ممحصاً وذا منهج مقارن ، وقد قام بمقارنة كل تلك العلوم الهندية التي وصل إلى معرفتها معرفة تكاد تكون تامة ، من طب وفلك ورياضيات بتراث اليونان العلمي ، ثم قارن كل هذا بما عند المسلمين . ولقد وصل البيروني إلى النتائج الخطيرة الآتية : أنه كان لدى الهنود علم جزئي كبير ، على درجة من التقدم ، لكن لا يربطه رباط علمي أو منهجي ، أبحاث متناثرة في الطب والرياضيات والفلكيات والطبيعيات، ولكن خالية من الإطار المنهجي الرائع . وكان لدى اليونان - على العكس من هذا - نظرية العلم ، نظرية البرهان . فبينما لا نجد لدى اليونان هذا العدد الجديد من أبحاث الهنود في مختلف العلوم الجزئية ، التي كان الهنود سادتها ، لا نجد لدى الهنود نظرية في العلم ، في البرهان دون المقدمات اليقينية ، قمة الفكر اليوناني . وسمة الحضارة اليونانية كلها . وكان الهنود يقفون منثالين متعجبين مسحورين ، حين يعرض لهم هذه النظرية الأخيرة ، بل إنهم نسبوه إلى السحر(١) . هذه النتائج الخطيرة التي توصل إليها هذا العالم المنهجي ، الذي كان له أثره الكبير في تطور العلم الإسلامي ، بمنهجه الإستقرائي الرائع كما كان له فضله العظيم في تاريخ العلوم عامة . ولكن البيروني لم يتنبه إلى فضله هو وفضل أسلافه من علماء التطبيق المسلمين إلى أن منهج المسلمين لم يكن هذا ولا ذاك . فلا هم شغلوا بالعلم الجزئي فحسب ، كما شغل الهنود ولم يشغلوا بالنظر ـ وآلته البرهان ، كما شغل اليونان ، وإنما توصلوا إلى المنهج الإستقرائي التجريبي كمنهج ، وطبقوه على علوم الهنود وعلوم اليونان .
على أية حال : كانت الفكرة الشائعة عن دخول العلم أنه أتى من خارج . وذهب مؤرخو العلم الإنساني إلى أن العرب تناولوا هذه العلوم بالأخذ أو بالتعديل أو بالنقد . وإذا كان مؤرخو العلم من الأوروبيين ، أنكروا فضل العرب الفلسفي ، فإنهم لم يستطيعوا على الإطلاق إنكار فضلهم العلمي ـ لكن على أساس أنه نتيجة لعلوم اليونان خاصة . ونحن نعلم أن أفكار الحسن بن الهيثم عاشت في أوروبا إلى زمان ليس ببعيد عنا ، كما نعلم أن أبحاث الطوسي في الرياضيات وتناوله لهندسة أقليدس ومصادراته ، بقيت زمناً طويلاً يتناولها علماء أوروبا ، كما نعلم وأن كتاب ابن سينا الطبي ـ القانون ، بقي المرجع الأساسي لكليات الطب في أوروبا حتى القرن السابع عشر ، وما زالت عناية الباحثين بالعلم العربي قائمة على أشدها ، يعنون بمكانته في التراث العلمي وقد وجه الأنظار إلى قيمة هذا العلم مؤرخ تاريخ العلم الإنساني : الأستاذ سارتون .
ولكن ينبغي أن نلاحظ أن السبب الأساسي في تطور العلم عند العرب ، أنهم قابلوه بروح جديدة في البحث كانت هي السبب الهام في تطوره ، ودفعه دفعة قوية إلى الأيام ، ولولا هذه « الدفعة » لما عاش ، ولما اعترف به مؤرخو العلم الأوروبيون أدنى اعتراف . لقد كان بيدهم المنهج الإستقرائي ، الذي اكتشفوه كما رأينا كاملاً . وقد رأينا حركة اكتشافه لدى مفكري الإسلام . وقد انتقل هذا المنهج إلى دوائر العلماء .
The medical and scientific educational council moved from Alexandria in the era of the Umayyad caliph Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz to Antioch, then to Harran, then to Marv, taking a long journey that ended in Baghdad. Then the scientific assemblies in Baghdad began the translation movement on one hand, and scientific research on the other. The Abbasid caliphs summoned these translators to their palaces, and al-Ma'mun later built the House of Wisdom; and experimental research began in various sciences.
So we can say: The history of science among the Arabs begins with the migration of the Alexandrian Greek library to Baghdad, then followed by another scientific migration from the lands of the non-Arabs – ancient Persia – carrying to the Arab world the science of Iran – then a third migration came from India and Sindh carrying many opinions of the Indians in medicine, astronomy, and mathematics. We will later see a great scholar, al-Biruni, speaking in his book "Taḥqīq mā li-l-Hind min maqūla" about the sciences of India. Al-Biruni – as far as we know – was a thorough scholar with a comparative methodology. He compared all those Indian sciences that he had come to know almost completely – medicine, astronomy, mathematics – with the Greek scientific heritage, then compared all this with what the Muslims had. Al-Biruni reached the following serious results: That the Indians had a great partial science, with a degree of advancement, but nothing binds it scientifically or methodologically – scattered researches in medicine, mathematics, astronomy, and physics – but devoid of the magnificent methodological framework. The Greeks – on the contrary – had the theory of science, the theory of demonstration. While we do not find among the Greeks this large number of Indian researches in various partial sciences, in which the Indians were masters, we do not find among the Indians a theory of science, of demonstration based on certain premises – the peak of Greek thought and the hallmark of the entire Greek civilization. The Indians stood amazed, astonished, spellbound, when this latter theory was presented to them; indeed they attributed it to magic. These serious results reached by this methodical scholar, who had a great impact on the development of Islamic science with his magnificent inductive method, and who had great merit in the history of sciences in general. But al-Biruni did not pay attention to his own merit and that of his predecessors among the practical Muslim scholars – that the method of the Muslims was not this nor that. They were not occupied solely with partial science, as the Indians were occupied, nor were they occupied with theory – and its instrument, demonstration – as the Greeks were occupied; rather they arrived at the empirical inductive method as a method, and applied it to the sciences of the Indians and the sciences of the Greeks.
In any case: The prevalent idea about the entry of science was that it came from outside. Historians of human science held that the Arabs took these sciences either by adoption, modification, or criticism. If the historians of science among the Europeans denied the philosophical merit of the Arabs, they were absolutely unable to deny their scientific merit – but on the basis that it is a result especially of the Greek sciences. We know that the ideas of al-Hasan ibn al-Haytham lived in Europe until a time not far from us, and that the researches of al-Tusi in mathematics and his handling of Euclid's geometry and his postulates remained for a long time dealt with by European scholars, and that Ibn Sina's medical book – al-Qānūn – remained the primary reference for medical faculties in Europe until the seventeenth century. The attention of researchers to Arabic science is still at its strongest, concerned with its place in the scientific heritage, and the historian of human science, Professor Sarton, has drawn attention to the value of this science.
But it should be noted that the basic reason for the development of science among the Arabs is that they met it with a new spirit in research, which was the important reason for its development and gave it a strong push forward. Without this 'push', it would not have lived, nor would European historians of science have accorded it any recognition. They had in their hands the inductive method, which they discovered completely as we have seen. And we have seen the movement of its discovery among the thinkers of Islam. And this method was transferred to the circles of scholars.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage is a historical introduction that traces the transmission of scientific knowledge from Greek, Persian, and Indian sources to the Islamic world, culminating in the development of a uniquely Islamic empirical inductive method. The author begins by describing the migration of the Alexandrian medical and scientific council through various cities to Baghdad, sparking translation and research. He then outlines three great migrations: the Greek library, Persian science, and Indian medicine/astronomy/mathematics. The focus then shifts to al-Biruni's comparative methodology: he compared Indian sciences (medicine, astronomy, mathematics) with Greek and Islamic sciences. Al-Biruni's key finding was that Indian sciences were extensive but lacked a systematic methodological framework, whereas Greek sciences had a theory of demonstration (burhān) but lacked the volume of empirical detail. The author then critiques al-Biruni for not recognizing that the Muslim method was neither purely empirical (like Indians) nor purely theoretical (like Greeks), but a synthesis: the empirical inductive method (al-manhaj al-istiqrā'ī al-tajrībī). The passage continues by discussing how European historians often attribute Arab scientific achievements to Greek influence, yet examples like Ibn al-Haytham's optics, al-Ṭūsī's mathematics, and Ibn Sīnā's Canon show lasting independent contributions. The author concludes that the essential reason for the advancement of Islamic science was the inductive method, which Muslims discovered and applied, giving science a powerful push that ensured its survival and recognition.

Key Terms
نظرية البرهان
علم جزئي

A) They adopted it immediately.

C) They found it inferior to their own methods.
D) They ignored it completely.

Key Takeaway
Al-Biruni found that Greek science had a strong theoretical framework but lacked empirical depth, while Indian science had empirical richness but no systematic methodology.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
5 min
The Muslim Empirical Inductive Method
Author describes the Muslim empirical inductive method as a synthesis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the Muslim empirical inductive method (al-manhaj al-istiqrāʾī al-tajrībī) synthesized Greek and Indian approaches according to the author.

Core Idea
Muslims did not just adopt—they innovated a new method: empirical induction.

The author argues that the unique contribution of Islamic science was not merely borrowing from Greeks or Indians, but the development of an empirical inductive method that combined systematic observation with theoretical demonstration. This method was applied across sciences and gave a strong push to scientific progress.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's critique of al-Biruni
Find where the author says al-Biruni did not recognize his own merit. The author claims that the Muslim method was neither purely Indian (partial science) nor purely Greek (theory alone), but a synthesis.
2
Extract the definition of the Muslim method
Look for the phrase 'al-manhaj al-istiqrāʾī al-tajrībī' (empirical inductive method). The author says Muslims 'discovered' this method completely.
3
Identify the application of this method
The passage mentions that this method was applied to both Indian and Greek sciences. Also later examples: Ibn al-Haytham, al-Ṭūsī, Ibn Sīnā.
These scholars used empirical observation combined with theoretical reasoning.
4
Explain the significance
The author concludes that this inductive method was the 'essential reason' for the development of science among the Arabs, giving science a strong push forward.
Without this push, European historians would not have recognized it.
